Proof That Accidents Are Not Eternal
Nasafi enumerates a trilemma against the eternity of accidents and refutes each branch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Nasafi's trilemma argument that accidents cannot be eternal because their non-existence is observed, which would be impossible if they were eternal in themselves.

Core Idea
If accidents were eternal, they could never cease—but they do.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi presents a rigorous proof that accidents (non-substantial properties like color, smell, etc.) are not eternal in themselves. He begins with a general principle about the ease of preventing versus removing, then poses a decisive trilemma to those who claim accidents can persist forever. By examining when a temporal opposite could cause the non-existence of an eternal accident, he shows each possibility leads to an impossibility. The conclusion is inescapable: accidents are not eternal, and their observed succession confirms this.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the tight logical progression.
2
Identify the trilemma
The passage presents three possibilities for when a temporal (new) accident could cause the non-existence of an eternal accident: before, during, or after its existence. Find these three and see how each is refuted.
This is the backbone of the argument.
3
Understand the locus condition
Pay attention to the discussion about the locus (maḥall). The argument hinges on whether the new accident exists in the same locus as the eternal one, a different locus, or no locus at all. Each option is impossible.
Accidents cannot transfer between loci.
4
Reflect on the consequence
After the refutations, note the conclusion: if accidents were eternal in themselves, their non-existence would be inconceivable. Since we observe opposites succeeding each other, they cannot be eternal.
This is a direct empirical refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يرفع الثابت المتقرر ، والباقي يرفع ما لم يتقرر بعد ولم يثبت بعد، ولا شك أن دفع ما لم يثبت أسهل من رفع ما تقرر ، فكان رفع الثابت المتقرر لما لم يثبت بعد أولى في عقل من له عقل من رفع ما ليس بثابت في نفسه لما هو ثابت متقرر. والذي يقطع شغب هؤلاء الجهلة أن يقال لهم : إن الطارئ متى يوجب عدم الباقي قبل وجوده أم في حال وجوده أم بعدما وجد ؟ لا جائز أن يقال يوجب عدم الباقي قبل وجوده لأنه قبل وجوده معدوم، والمعدوم لا يوجب عدم الموجود، فكان انعدام الباقي قبل وجود الطارئ لعلة سوى وجود الطارئ، ولا جائز أن يوجب عدمه في حال وجوده لأنا نقول : يوجد في هذا المحل أم لا في محل (أم في محل) آخر؟ فإن قال : يوجد في هذا، فهذا محال، لأن وجوده في هذا المحل (قبل ارتفاع الأول عن المحل محال؛ لأنه يوجب البقاء للضدين في الوجود، وإذا التقيا في الوجود لم يكونا ضدين وإن وجد لا في محل (فوجود العرض لا في محل) محال أيضا، ووجوده لا في محل هذا الضد لا يوجب عدمه؛ لأن وجوده ضد لا في محل ضده لا يوجب عدم ضده إن وجد في محل آخر، فذاك لا يوجب عدم العرض الباقي لأن السواد الموجود في المحل لا يوجب عدم البياض الثابت في محل آخر إلى أن ينتقل إلى هذا المحل، ولا يتصور انتفاء له إلى هذا المحل لاستحالة الانتقال من محل إلى محل على الأعراض، ولأن هذا العرض الطارئ عندهم (عندهم) علة لانعدام الباقي، ومن أصلهم أن العلة تكون سابقة على المعلول، فيكون وجود الطارئ سابقا على انعدام الموجود ثم لا يوجب عدمه ما لم ينتقل إليه لما مر، فينتقل إليه ثم يعدمه في الثاني فيؤدي إلى اجتماع الضدين في محل الضد الأول، ولا جائز أن يوجب عدمه في الثاني لأنا نقول: وجد في أي محل حتى يوجب عدم العرض الباقي عن المحل في الثاني، ويقسم الكلام ويبطل كل قسم على ما ذكرنا في الفصل المتقدم؟ ولأنه لو جاز وجودهما في زمان واحد جاز أيضا في الثاني والثالث إذ ليس تنافيهما في الثاني أولى من تنافيهما في الأول، ولأن عدم الباقي في الثاني لوجود ضده ليس بأولى من عدم ضده في الثاني لوجوده، والحالة الثانية لكل واحد منهما حالة البقاء، فثبت بما قررنا أن العرض لو كان باقيًا لذاته لما تصور عدمه، وحيث رأينا تعاقب الأعراض المتضادة على المحال شاهدة علمنا بطلان قولهم ضرورة
He removes the established and confirmed, and the remaining removes what has not yet been established and has not yet been confirmed. There is no doubt that repelling what has not been established is easier than removing what has been established. So removing the established and confirmed for what has not yet been established is more appropriate in the mind of one who has intellect than removing what is not established in itself for what is established and confirmed. And what cuts off the clamor of these ignoramuses is to say to them: When does the temporal (al-ṭāriʾ) cause the non-existence of the remaining (al-bāqī): before its existence, during its existence, or after it exists? It is not permissible to say that it causes the non-existence of the remaining before its existence, because before its existence it is non-existent, and the non-existent does not cause the non-existence of the existent. So the non-existence of the remaining before the existence of the temporal is due to a cause other than the existence of the temporal. And it is not permissible that it causes its non-existence during its existence, for we say: Does it exist in this locus (maḥall) or in a different locus? If he says: It exists in this [locus], then this is impossible, because its existence in this locus before the removal of the first from the locus is impossible, since it necessitates the persistence of two opposites in existence. If they meet in existence, they would not be opposites. And if it exists without a locus, then the existence of an accident without a locus is also impossible. And its existence without a locus of this opposite does not cause its non-existence, because the existence of an opposite without the locus of its opposite does not cause the non-existence of its opposite if it exists in another locus. So that does not cause the non-existence of the remaining accident, because blackness existing in a locus does not cause the non-existence of whiteness established in another locus until it transfers to this locus. And its transfer to this locus is inconceivable due to the impossibility of transfer from locus to locus for accidents. And because this temporal accident, according to them, is a cause (ʿilla) for the non-existence of the remaining. And it is a principle of theirs that the cause precedes the effect. So the existence of the temporal would be prior to the non-existence of the existent. Then it does not cause its non-existence as long as it has not transferred to it, for what has passed, so it transfers to it, then it annihilates it in the second [moment], leading to the conjunction of two opposites in the locus of the first opposite. And it is not permissible that it causes its non-existence in the second, for we say: In which locus did it exist so that it causes the non-existence of the remaining accident from the locus in the second? And he divides the statement and refutes each division as we mentioned in the preceding chapter? And because if their existence in one time were permissible, it would also be permissible in the second and third, since their mutual exclusion in the second is not more appropriate than their mutual exclusion in the first. And because the non-existence of the remaining in the second due to the existence of its opposite is not more appropriate than the non-existence of its opposite in the second due to its existence. And the second state for each of them is a state of persistence. So it is established by what we have set forth that if an accident were persistent (bāqiyan) in itself, its non-existence would be inconceivable. Since we see the successive occurrence of opposite accidents upon loci, witnessing, we know the falsehood of their statement necessarily.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a proof that accidents (aʿrāḍ) are not eternal (bāqiya) in themselves. It begins by establishing a general principle: it is easier to prevent something not yet established than to remove something already established. The author argues that if accidents were eternal, their non-existence would be more difficult to explain. He then presents a decisive refutation against those who claim accidents can be eternal by posing a question: When does a temporal event (al-ṭāriʾ) cause the non-existence of an eternal accident? Three possibilities are examined: before the temporal event exists, during its existence, or after it exists. The first is impossible because a non-existent thing cannot cause non-existence. The second is impossible because if the temporal event exists in the same locus as the eternal accident, they would coexist as opposites, which is impossible; if it exists in a different locus, it cannot affect the other accident. The third possibility leads to an infinite regress and the impossibility of transferring accidents between loci. The argument concludes that if an accident were persistent in itself, its non-existence would be inconceivable. Since we observe opposite accidents (e.g., black and white) succeeding each other in loci, we know that they are not eternal.

Key Terms
باقي (bāqī)
طارئ (ṭāriʾ)
ضدان (ḍiddān)
علة (ʿilla)
عرض (ʿaraḍ)
محل (maḥall)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the argument denies that God can annihilate accidents, or that it assumes accidents cannot cease at all.
Correction: The argument does not deny that God can annihilate accidents; it argues that if accidents were eternal by themselves (i.e., self-subsisting), they could not cease. Their observed ceasing proves they are contingent, not eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for a temporal event to cause the non-existence of an eternal accident before the temporal event exists?
A) Because the temporal event does not yet exist and a non-existent cannot cause non-existence.
B) Because the eternal accident is stronger than the temporal event.
C) Because the temporal event exists in a different locus.
D) Because the temporal event is a cause that must precede its effect.

Key Takeaway
The observed succession of opposite accidents proves that accidents are not eternal, because if they were, their non-existence would be inconceivable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You witness the constant succession of opposite accidents in your own body and surroundings—your health fades, your moods shift, your possessions change. What are you holding onto as if it were permanent, when in reality it is just a passing accident that cannot persist by itself? Are you building your life on something that can disappear at any moment?
?
This lesson proves that nothing in creation—not even the smallest quality—can remain by itself. How does that make you feel about your own existence? Are you living as though you are self-sufficing, or as though every moment you depend on the One who sustains you?
?
If you truly internalized that every accidental quality you possess—your health, wealth, beauty, knowledge, even your thoughts—is fleeting and not self-subsisting, what would you change today about your priorities, your worries, or your attachments? What would you begin to let go of right now?

The Opponents' Dilemma on the Origin of Persistence
Presents the opponent's dilemma on the origin of persistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' dilemma that challenges the Maturidi view on the attribute of persistence (baqa').

Core Idea
The opponents force a choice: persistence originated at the first moment or the second — both problematic.

In this passage, the author presents a key objection from those who deny that persistence requires a separate attribute. They argue that if persistence were an attribute, it would have to be originated either at the same instant as the substance or later, leading to contradictions. This lesson examines the dilemma itself, setting the stage for the Maturidi response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the structure of the dilemma.
The Arabic uses conditional statements and logical alternatives.
2
Identify the two horns
Notice the two possibilities: persistence originated with the substance (first moment) or in the second moment.
The phrase 'حادثا في حال حدوثه أو في الثاني' marks the alternatives.
3
Understand the implications
For each horn, the opponent points out a contradiction: if in the first, persistence is redundant; if in the second, it fails to explain persistence from the start.
The argument assumes persistence must be an originated accident.
4
Consider the context
This is a standard objection in kalam. The Maturidi answer will be presented in later lessons.
The dilemma is not answered here, so focus on understanding the opponent's logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نذكر أسئلتهم التي يعولون عليها في إبطال قولنا، فمن ذلك قولهم: إن الباقي لو كان باقيًا ببقاء لم يخل البقاء من أن يكون حادثا في حال حدوثه أو في الثاني، فإن حدث في حال حدوث الجوهر وجب أن يكون باقيا فيه لوجود البقاء له، وإن كان حادثا في الثاني لم يبق به من الأول.
Then we mention their questions upon which they rely in invalidating our statement. Among them is their statement: If the persisting thing were persistent by a persistence, then that persistence would not be free from being originated in the state of its occurrence or in the second. If it occurs in the state of the occurrence of the substance, then it must be persistent in it due to the existence of persistence for it. And if it were originated in the second, it would not persist by it from the first.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the opponents (likely Mu'tazilites) against the Maturidi view that persistence requires a separate attribute (baqa'). The opponent argues by dilemma: if a substance persists by a persistence (an attribute of persistence), then that persistence must be originated either at the moment the substance originates (first moment) or in a subsequent moment (second moment). If in the first moment, then the substance already persists in that first moment, so persistence is redundant. If in the second moment, then the substance did not persist from the first moment, contradicting the claim that it persists from the beginning. This objection challenges the need for a distinct attribute of persistence. The answer to this objection is not provided in the current window, so the lesson presents the objection only.

Key Terms
بقاء
حادث
أول
ثاني
جوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the dilemma is unanswerable, showing that the attribute of persistence cannot exist.
Correction: Maturidis respond by distinguishing between the attribute of persistence and the thing's own existence, offering a nuanced answer.

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma posed by the opponents regarding the attribute of persistence (baqa')?
A) Whether persistence is a substance or accident.
B) Whether persistence is originated in the first or second moment.
C) Whether persistence can be seen.
D) Whether persistence is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The opponents argue that if persistence were an attribute, it would face a logical dilemma regarding its origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a logical dilemma about your faith, do you feel compelled to choose one problematic option, or do you actively seek a third possibility? Reflect on a time you faced such a dilemma. What did you do? Will you now train yourself to think more critically before accepting forced choices?
?
The opponents demanded that every attribute must have a precise temporal origin. Have you ever demanded that God's actions fit perfectly into your logical framework? What would it cost you to humbly admit that His ways may surpass your understanding?
?
This passage shows that even objections to belief can be analyzed with meticulous logic. Examine your own habits: do you dismiss critiques of your faith with emotion, or do you engage them rigorously? Make a concrete commitment to study one intellectual challenge to your aqeedah this week.

The Objection on Motion and the Unified Answer
Nasafi presents an atheist objection on motion and the answer equating motion and permanence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the atheist dilemma regarding motion as an accident and the early kalām answer that equates motion with permanence in terms of their occurrence in the second location/time.

Core Idea
Motion occurs in the second place, just as permanence occurs in the second time.

This passage presents a sophisticated dilemma raised by atheists against the reality of motion as an accident. Imam Nasafi then relates an answer from some early theologians, including Abu al-Hudhayl and al-Ash'ari, who solve the dilemma by equating motion with permanence — both arise anew at the destination, not through transition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First read the Arabic text and its translation to get the full flow of the objection and the answer. Note the dilemma structure and the parallel drawn between motion and permanence.
The passage includes a footnote marker (۱) which is part of the original text.
2
Identify the atheist dilemma
Find the two horns of the dilemma: if motion occurs in the first place, the body has motion but doesn't move; if it occurs in the second place, the body is already there so motion is unnecessary.
This is a classic argument against accidents in kalām.
3
Understand the unified answer
Focus on the answer: motion happens in the second place, just as permanence happens in the second time. The body's exit from the first place negates its being there, so it needs a new 'location' (kawn) in the second place.
The answer redefines motion as an accident occurring at the destination, not en route.
4
Note the attribution
The passage attributes this view to Abu al-Hudhayl, a group of Mu'tazila, and al-Ash'ari. This shows the answer crossed theological schools.
This indicates a shared solution among early kalām thinkers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عنه : أن هذا سؤال مأخوذ من سؤال الملحدين في نفي الأعراض، وهو قولهم : لو كان الجسم متحركاً بحركة لم تخل حركتها من أن تكون حادثة في المكان الأول. أو تكون حادثة وهو في المكان الثاني، فإن حدثت الحركة والجسم (۱) في المكان الأول لكانت الحركة موجودة والجسم ليس بمتحرك ولم يجب خروجه عن مكانه لوجودها، وإن حدثت وهي في المكان الثاني استغن بكونه فيه عن حركة ينتقل بها إليه. ثم من أصحابنا من أجاب عن السؤالين جميعًا بجواب وحد وسوى بين الحركة والبقاء وقال : إن الحركة تحدث والجسم في المكان الثاني والبقاء يحدث في الوقت الثاني، وإنما كان كذلك لأن المتحرك إنما يكون متحركا بخروجه عن الأول وكونه في الثاني، واحتاج إلى حدوث الكون فيه لأن خروجه عن الأول نفى لكونه فيه ولا يحتاج إلى الكون في مكان ليس بكائن فيه. وهذا المذهب في الحركة مذهب أبي الهذيل وجماعة من المعتزلة، وإليه ذهب الأشعري. والباقي يكون باقيًا في الوقت الثاني واحتاج إلى البقاء فيه وليس ببقاء عن الأول إلى الثاني، وإنما يكون محدثًا في الأول غير باق ثم يكون باقيًا في الثاني بحدوث البقاء فيه.
And the answer to it: that this is a question taken from the question of the atheists in denying accidents, which is their statement: 'If the body were moving with a motion, its motion would not be free from either being originated in the first place, or being originated while it is in the second place. For if the motion occurred while the body (۱) was in the first place, then the motion would exist while the body is not moving, and its exit from its place would not be necessitated by its existence. And if it occurred while it is in the second place, then by its being in it, it would be independent of a motion by which to transfer to it.' Then some of our companions answered both questions with a single answer and equated motion and permanence, saying: 'Motion occurs while the body is in the second place, and permanence occurs in the second time. It is only thus because the moving thing is only moving by its exit from the first and its being in the second, and it needed the occurrence of location (al-kawn) in it because its exit from the first negated its being in it, and it does not need location in a place in which it is not existing.' And this doctrine regarding motion is the doctrine of Abu al-Hudhayl and a group of the Mu'tazila, and al-Ash'ari held it. And the remaining thing is remaining in the second time and needed permanence in it, and it is not a permanence from the first to the second, but rather it is originated in the first, not remaining, then it becomes remaining in the second by the occurrence of permanence in it.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from atheists regarding the existence of motion as an accident, followed by a specific answer from some of the author's school (Ashabuna) who equate motion and permanence. The objection is a dilemma: if a body moves, its motion must occur either in the first location (before movement begins) or in the second location (after movement is complete). If in the first, the body would have motion without actually moving, so exit from place is not necessary. If in the second, the body already occupies the second place, so motion is unnecessary. The answer posits that motion occurs in the second place, just as permanence occurs in the second time. The explanation is that a moving thing becomes moving by exiting the first and entering the second; the occurrence of location (kawn) in the second is needed because exit from the first negated the first location. This view is attributed to Abu al-Hudhayl, some Mu'tazila, and al-Ash'ari. The parallel is drawn with permanence: a thing persists in the second time by a new permanence, not by continuation from the first.

بقاء
كون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the dilemma proves motion cannot exist, but the answer shows it exists as an accident in the second location.
Correction: The answer does not deny motion; it redefines its occurrence as happening at the destination, analogous to permanence.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer to the objection on motion, where does motion occur?
A) In the first place
B) In the second place
C) Simultaneously in both places
D) Neither place

Key Takeaway
The unified answer resolves the atheist dilemma by positing motion as an accident occurring in the second place, just as permanence occurs anew in the second time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Motion as Two Consecutive Accidents
Imam Nasafi presents the view that motion consists of two locations (kawnān).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the view that motion is not a single accident but two consecutive 'locations' (kawnān), and who held this view.

Core Idea
Motion is the completion of two consecutive accidents, not a single moving accident.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi presents a significant alternative view on motion from those who distinguish between motion and permanence. They argue that 'motion' is not a single accident but the name for two accidents: two 'locations' (kawnān). This view was held by Ibn al-Rawandi and Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi among others. Let's explore this position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice how the author presents the distinction between motion and permanence.
The passage is a response to an objection about motion.
2
Identify the two accidents
The passage states that motion is not a single accident but two: one in the first place (al-makān al-awwal) and one in the second place (al-makān al-thānī). These are called 'locations' (kawnān).
3
Understand the completion argument
Motion is not present in either place individually; it is completed (tamma) in the second place. Until the second location exists, the body is not moving.
This refutes the idea that motion is a single accident residing in the body.
4
Note the proponents
The passage names Ibn al-Rawandi (in his later years) and Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi and a group of theologians as holding this view.
Ibn al-Rawandi is a controversial figure; his later repentance is mentioned.
5
Consider the broader context
This is one of several answers to the problem of motion. Reflect on how this view differs from saying motion is a single accident.
The author says the matter will become clearer later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من فرق بين الحركة والبقاء، وأجاب عن سؤال الحركة أن ليس باسم لعرض واحد بل هو اسم لعرضين وهما كونان أحدهما في المكان الأول، والآخر في المكان الثاني، فما لم يوجد الثاني لا يكون متحركًا فلا تكون الحركة موجودة في المكان الأول ولا في الثاني بل يتم في الثاني، وإليه ذهب ابن الراوندي في آخر عمره، وهو مذهب أبي العباس القلانسي وجماعة من المتكلمين: يتبين ذلك بعد هذا إن شاء الله تعالى.
And among them are those who distinguished between motion and permanence, and answered the question of motion that it is not the name of a single accident but rather it is the name of two accidents, which are two locations (kawnān): one in the first place and the other in the second place. As long as the second does not exist, one is not moving, so motion does not exist in the first place nor in the second, but rather it is completed in the second. And Ibn al-Rawandi held this view at the end of his life, and it is the doctrine of Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi and a group of the theologians (mutakallimūn). This will become clear after this, God willing.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents a second answer to the objection on motion, from those who differentiate motion from permanence. They argue that 'motion' is not a single accident but the name for two accidents: two 'locations' (kawnān) – one in the first place and one in the second. Until the second location exists, the body is not moving, so motion is not present in either place individually but is completed in the second. This view is attributed to Ibn al-Rawandi (in his later years) and to Abu al-Abbas al-Qalanisi and a group of theologians. The author notes that this will become clearer later.

Abu Mansur's Unified Account of Motion and Rest
Imam Nasafi transmits Abu Mansur's view that motion is departure (زوال) and rest is settlement (قرار).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abu Mansur defines motion and rest as a single accident of location (kawn) distinguished only by context.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are the same accident, differing only by the preceding location.

How do we distinguish between a moving object and a stationary one? Abu Mansur al-Maturidi offers a surprisingly elegant answer: motion and rest are not two different things—they are the same accident of 'being in a place' (kawn), labeled based on what came before. This passage presents his view and the reasoning behind it, showing how a single underlying reality takes on different names depending on context.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: زوال (departure), قرار (settlement), كون (location/accident of being).
The footnotes (1) and (2) are part of the original text.
2
Understand Abu Mansur's definitions
Identify the two definitions: motion = departure, rest = settlement. Then see how the verifiers interpret this as leaning toward the 'first doctrine'.
3
Follow the argument against two concurrent locations
Tracing the reasoning: if motion or rest required two locations simultaneously, no object could move or rest. This is because two accidents of location cannot exist in one instant.
This reveals the principle of a single kawn per state.
4
Grasp the contextual distinction
The final sentence shows that the same single location is called departure or settlement based solely on the previous location—different place yields motion, same place yields rest.
This is the core insight.
5
Reflect and test understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you can apply this distinction. Then consider the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثير ما يذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور وأكثر عباراته في هذا: أن الحركة زوال والسكون قرار ، وهو عند المحققين ميل إلى المذهب الأول إذ الزوال كون واحد وهو الحاصل في المكان الثاني إذ به زال عن الأول، وكذا القرار كون واحد إذ به صار مستقرا ، ولو كان السكون كونين وكذا الحركة لما تصور كون ذات ما متحركًا ولا ساكنا لاستحالة وجود كونين (۱) في حالة واحدة فإذا لا يوجد في كل حال إلا كون واحد غير أنه إن كان قبله كون آخر في مكان آخر كان الكون الثاني زوالاً ، وإن (۲) كان قبله كون آخر في هذا المكان فهو قرار.
And often what al-Shaykh Abu Mansur inclines to, and most of his expressions on this: that motion is departure (zawāl) and rest is settlement (qarār). And according to the verifiers, this is an inclination toward the first doctrine, because departure is a single location (kawn wāḥid) which obtains in the second place, since by it one departs from the first. Likewise, settlement is a single location, since by it one becomes settled. And if rest were two locations, and likewise motion, it would not be conceivable for a thing to be moving or resting, due to the impossibility of two locations (１) existing in a single state. So in every state there exists only one location, except that if before it there was another location in another place, the second location is departure; and if before it there was another location in this same place, it is settlement.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage presents the view of Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (the author's teacher) on motion and rest. He defines motion as departure (zawāl) and rest as settlement (qarār). The verifiers (muḥaqqiqūn) consider this a leaning toward the first doctrine (the unified answer), because departure is a single location that occurs in the second place by which one leaves the first. Similarly, settlement is a single location by which one becomes settled. If rest or motion required two locations simultaneously, it would be impossible for a thing to be moving or resting since two locations cannot exist in one state. Therefore, in any given state only one location exists. The characterization depends on the preceding location: if preceded by a location in a different place, the current location is departure (motion); if preceded by a location in the same place, it is settlement (rest).

Key Terms
زوال
قرار
كون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion and rest are two fundamentally different types of accidents.
Correction: Abu Mansur argues they are the same accident (kawn) distinguished only by the preceding location: departure if preceded by a different place, settlement if preceded by the same place.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Mansur, what distinguishes motion from rest?
A) Motion has two accidents, rest has one
B) The same single location is called 'departure' or 'settlement' based on the preceding location
C) Motion exists in two places simultaneously
D) Rest is the absence of motion

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest are not distinct accidents but the same single location (kawn), named according to whether the preceding location was different (motion) or the same (rest).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, every moment of your existence is a single kawn that is either 'departure' (if you moved) or 'settlement' (if you stayed). Consider the last hour: how many kawns did you experience? Were they mostly departures or settlements? What does that say about the direction of your life? Now, ask yourself: are your movements carrying you closer to Allah or further away? Commit to consciously choosing your next kawn.

The lesson says that motion and rest are not separate but the same accident differing only by what came before. Think of a habit you consider a 'wrong' change (motion) or a 'healthy' stability (rest). Could it be that both are actually the same underlying reality—a moment of being—and your judgment depends on what preceded it? What would change if you viewed your actions not as good or bad types, but as single moments to be directed toward Allah? Will you treat your next action as a fresh kawn to be used for His sake?

Abu Mansur argues that if motion and rest were two distinct locations, a thing could never be moving or resting. Reflect on a time you felt stuck between two choices, unable to act. Did you assume that the 'right' and 'wrong' paths were two different realities? According to this lesson, your current state (kawn) is just one; your perception of it as 'progress' or 'stagnation' depends on where you were before. The Definition of Permanence as an Accident
Nasafi defines permanence as a single accident (duration) and explains its temporal scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Imam Nasafi defines permanence (baqā') as a single accident (duration) and clarifies that a thing is described as remaining only in the second moment of existence.

Core Idea
Permanence is a created accident, not the essence of a thing.

Imam Nasafi begins by establishing the precise meaning of permanence (al-baqā'). He tells us it is a single accident synonymous with duration and continuation. He then clarifies a temporal nuance: a thing is not said to be 'remaining' at the first instant of its existence, but only thereafter. This sets the stage for later arguments against those who deny that permanence is a created attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the short Arabic passage once. It defines permanence and gives its temporal condition. Note the two keywords: بقاء and دوام.
The passage is divided into two parts: definition and temporal scope.
2
Identify the definition of permanence
The passage says permanence is 'the name of a single accident, which is duration and the continuation of existence.' This tells us permanence is not the thing itself but an accident that describes it.
An accident (عرض) is a non-essential attribute that inheres in a substance.
3
Understand the temporal condition
Nasafi states: 'the first will not exist in the second, nor will it be remaining in the first time, but rather it will be remaining in the second.' This means a thing is not described as 'remaining' at the very first moment of its existence, only in subsequent moments.
This distinguishes permanence from existence itself.
4
Ask why this definition matters
Ponder: If permanence were not a created accident, then a thing would either persist by itself (which implies independence from Allah) or not need an attribute at all. Nasafi's definition sets up a foundation for refuting such views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما في البقاء فهو اسم لعرض واحد وهو الدوام (۳) واستمرار الوجود، ولن يوجد الأول في الثاني (ولا) يكون باقيًا في الوقت (٤) الأول بل يكون باقيًا في الثاني) (٥) . ومما سألوا نحو قولهم : إن الجسم لو كان باقيًا ببقاء يخلق له لجاز أن يكون موجودًا (٦) وقتين فأكثر، ولا يخلق له البقاء، ويخلق فيه من جنس العرض الذي كان موجودًا في حال الحدوث، ويوجد أوقاتاً غير باق؟ والجواب عنه أن يقال: إذا استحال وجوده وقتين لا يوصف بالبقاء استحال أن لا يخلق فيه المعنى الذي من الوصف له به كالجسم لما استحال تعريه عن الأعراض استحال وجوده أجله صح وقتًا ولا يخلق فيه شيء منه. ثم يعكس عليهم هذا السؤال فيقال : لو كان الجسم باقيًا لنفسه أو لا لنفسه أو لا لمعنى لجاز أن يكون موجودًا وقتين غير موصوف بهذا الوصف، ولما استحال ذلك عند من نفى البقاء بطل ما أوردوه من السؤال.
As for permanence (al-baqā'), it is the name of a single accident, which is duration (al-dawām) (۳) and the continuation of existence. And the first will not exist in the second, nor (ولا) will it be remaining in the first time (٤), but rather it will be remaining in the second (٥). And among what they asked is like their statement: 'If the body were remaining by a permanence created for it, it would be possible for it to exist (٦) for two times or more, and the permanence not be created for it, while there is created in it something of the same kind as the accident that existed in the state of origination, and it exists at times without remaining?' And the answer to it is to say: If it is impossible for it to exist for two times without being described by permanence, it is impossible that there not be created in it the meaning by which it is described, just as when it was impossible for the body to be stripped of accidents, it was impossible for it to exist at all at a time without something of them being created in it. Then he reverses this question against them, saying: If the body were remaining by itself, or not by itself, or not by a meaning, it would be permissible for it to exist for two times without being described by this description. And since that is impossible according to those who deny permanence, what they have raised of the question becomes void.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of permanence (baqā') as an accident. The author states that permanence is a single accident meaning duration and continuation of existence. A thing does not remain in the first time but only in the second. An objection is raised: if a body persists by a created permanence, it could theoretically exist for two times without that permanence being created, while something like the original accident is created. The answer is that if it is impossible for a body to exist for two times without being described by permanence, it is impossible for the meaning (i.e., permanence) by which it is described not to be created. This is analogous to a body's existence requiring accidents. Then the author reverses the objection: if the body persisted by itself (without a separate permanence), it could exist for two times without being described by permanence. Since that is impossible according to those who deny permanence, their objection is invalid.

Key Terms
بقاء
دوام
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think permanence is the same as existence itself or that a thing is 'remaining' from the very first instant.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that permanence is an accident distinct from existence, and a thing is not described as remaining in the first moment.

Checkpoint

B) It is a single accident meaning duration and continuation of existence
C) It is not an accident but a relation

Key Takeaway
Permanence is a created accident that describes a thing's duration, and it applies only from the second moment of existence.

The Objection and Answer on Permanence
Nasafi presents an objection regarding permanence, answers with an analogy, and then reverses the objection against the opponent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if permanence is created, a body could exist without it, and how Nasafi answers using an analogy and then reverses the objection.

Core Idea
Permanence must be created because a body cannot exist without it, just as it cannot exist without accidents.

After defining permanence as a created accident, Imam Nasafi addresses an objection: if a body persists by a created permanence, could it not theoretically exist for multiple times without that specific permanence being created? He gives a sophisticated answer using an analogy from the necessity of accidents. Then he cleverly reverses the very same objection against the opponent, showing that their own position leads to an impossibility, thus refuting their challenge.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objectors say: If a body remains by a created permanence, it could exist for two times without that permanence being created, while something like the original accident is created at each time. Read this part slowly.
The objection seems to suggest that permanence is superfluous.
2
Understand the answer with the analogy
Nasafi answers that if it is impossible for a body to exist for two times without being described by permanence, then it is impossible for the meaning (permanence) not to be created. He compares this to a body's impossibility of existing without any accidents at all.
The analogy shows that just as a body needs accidents, it also needs permanence.
3
Follow the reversal of the objection
Nasafi then reverses the objection: if a body persisted by itself (without a separate meaning), it could exist for two times without being described by permanence. Since that is impossible for those who deny permanence, their objection fails.
This demonstrates the inconsistency of the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما في البقاء فهو اسم لعرض واحد وهو الدوام (۳) واستمرار الوجود، ولن يوجد الأول في الثاني (ولا) يكون باقيًا في الوقت (٤) الأول بل يكون باقيًا في الثاني) (٥) . ومما سألوا نحو قولهم : إن الجسم لو كان باقيًا ببقاء يخلق له لجاز أن يكون موجودًا (٦) وقتين فأكثر، ولا يخلق له البقاء، ويخلق فيه من جنس العرض الذي كان موجودًا في حال الحدوث، ويوجد أوقاتاً غير باق؟ والجواب عنه أن يقال: إذا استحال وجوده وقتين لا يوصف بالبقاء استحال أن لا يخلق فيه المعنى الذي من الوصف له به كالجسم لما استحال تعريه عن الأعراض استحال وجوده أجله صح وقتًا ولا يخلق فيه شيء منه. ثم يعكس عليهم هذا السؤال فيقال : لو كان الجسم باقيًا لنفسه أو لا لنفسه أو لا لمعنى لجاز أن يكون موجودًا وقتين غير موصوف بهذا الوصف، ولما استحال ذلك عند من نفى البقاء بطل ما أوردوه من السؤال.
As for permanence (al-baqā'), it is the name of a single accident, which is duration (al-dawām) (۳) and the continuation of existence. And the first will not exist in the second, nor (ولا) will it be remaining in the first time (٤), but rather it will be remaining in the second (٥). And among what they asked is like their statement: 'If the body were remaining by a permanence created for it, it would be possible for it to exist (٦) for two times or more, and the permanence not be created for it, while there is created in it something of the same kind as the accident that existed in the state of origination, and it exists at times without remaining?' And the answer to it is to say: If it is impossible for it to exist for two times without being described by permanence, it is impossible that there not be created in it the meaning by which it is described, just as when it was impossible for the body to be stripped of accidents, it was impossible for it to exist at all at a time without something of them being created in it. Then he reverses this question against them, saying: If the body were remaining by itself, or not by itself, or not by a meaning, it would be permissible for it to exist for two times without being described by this description. And since that is impossible according to those who deny permanence, what they have raised of the question becomes void.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of permanence (baqā') as an accident. The author states that permanence is a single accident meaning duration and continuation of existence. A thing does not remain in the first time but only in the second. An objection is raised: if a body persists by a created permanence, it could theoretically exist for two times without that permanence being created, while something like the original accident is created. The answer is that if it is impossible for a body to exist for two times without being described by permanence, it is impossible for the meaning (i.e., permanence) by which it is described not to be created. This is analogous to a body's existence requiring accidents. Then the author reverses the objection: if the body persisted by itself (without a separate permanence), it could exist for two times without being described by permanence. Since that is impossible according to those who deny permanence, their objection is invalid.

Key Terms
بقاء
عرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if permanence is created, a body could exist without it as long as some accident is present.
Correction: Nasafi shows that the very existence of a body at a second time requires the accident of permanence; without it, the body cannot be described as remaining.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Imam Nasafi use in his answer?
A) The body cannot exist without a place
B) The body cannot exist without accidents
C) The body cannot exist without motion
D) The body cannot exist without color

Key Takeaway
Nasafi defends the createdness of permanence by showing that a body cannot exist for two instants without it, and he turns the objection back on the opponent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You assume that things around you persist by their own nature. But this lesson teaches that your own continued existence from one moment to the next requires a freshly created permanence from Allah. When was the last time you thanked Him for keeping you in being for even a single breath? Will you commit to a daily moment of gratitude for your very existence?
?
The opponent's objection seemed logically sound, yet Nasafi exposed its hidden flaw. Are there comfortable beliefs you hold that you have never subjected to serious scrutiny? What would it cost you to honestly examine them today?
?
If a body cannot last two instants without a created permanence, then every relationship, every possession, every achievement you have is sustained by Allah's direct action each moment. How would your treatment of those things change if you truly lived with that awareness—starting right now?

Motion as Departure and Rest as Settling
The author defines motion as departure and rest as settling, arguing they are single states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi defines motion and rest as single states (zawal and qarar) rather than composite events.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are not composite but single states: departure and settling.

In this passage, al-Maturidi presents a concise but foundational definition of motion and rest. He argues that each is a single state (kawn), not a composite of multiple states. This understanding is critical for subsequent discussions of change and accidents in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the key terms: حركة, سكون, زوال, قرار, كون.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage defines motion as departure (zawal) and rest as settling (qarar). Understand that each is considered a single state (kawn).
This is the core claim.
3
Understand the argument against compositeness
The author argues that if motion or rest were composed of two states, no entity could be moving or resting at a given moment, because two states cannot coexist in one condition.
The argument hinges on the impossibility of two states in a single condition.
4
Reflect on the final conditional
The passage concludes that in any condition there is only one state, which is either departure (if preceded by a state in another place) or settling (if preceded by a state in the same place).
This shows how the same state can be classified based on context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكثير ما يذهب إليه الشيخ أبو منصور وأكثر عباراته في هذا: أن الحركة زوال والسكون قرار ، وهو عند المحققين ميل إلى المذهب الأول إذ الزوال كون واحد وهو الحاصل في المكان الثاني إذ به زال عن الأول، وكذا القرار كون واحد إذ به صار مستقرا ، ولو كان السكون كونين وكذا الحركة لما تصور كون ذات ما متحركًا ولا ساكنا لاستحالة وجود كونين في حالة واحدة فإذا لا يوجد في كل حال إلا كون واحد غير أنه إن كان قبله كون آخر في مكان آخر كان الكون الثاني زوالاً ، وإن كان قبله كون آخر في هذا المكان فهو قرار.
And many of what Abu Mansur (al-Maturidi) holds and most of his expressions in this: that motion (haraka) is departure (zawal) and rest (sukun) is settling (qarar). And it is, according to the verifiers, an inclination towards the first doctrine, since departure is a single state (kawn) which is attained in the second place because by it he departed from the first. Likewise settling is a single state because by it he became settled. And if rest were two states and likewise motion, then it would not be conceivable for an entity to be moving or resting due to the impossibility of two states existing in a single condition. Therefore, there exists in every condition only one state, except that if before it there was another state in another place, then the second state is departure; and if before it there was another state in this place, then it is settling.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage defines motion and rest. The author, following Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, defines motion as departure (zawal) and rest as settling (qarar). He explains that according to the verifiers (muhaqqiqun), this is an inclination toward the first doctrine, meaning that motion and rest are each a single state (kawn). Departure is the single state by which an entity leaves its first location, and settling is the single state by which it becomes settled. If rest or motion were composed of two states, it would be impossible for an entity to be moving or resting at a given moment because two states cannot coexist in one condition. Therefore, in any given condition, there is only one state. If that state is preceded by another state in a different place, it is departure; if preceded by another state in the same place, it is settling.

كون

Misconception: Motion and rest are composite events consisting of multiple states.
Correction: The author argues each is a single state; if they were two states, no entity could be moving or resting at a moment.

Checkpoint

A) Absence of motion
B) Settling (qarar)
C) Two states
D) Departure (zawal)

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest are each a single state (kawn): motion is departure from a previous place, rest is settling in the same place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that in every condition there is only one state (kawn), determined solely by what preceded it. Consider your current spiritual state right now—are you truly in a state of departure from sin and moving toward Allah, or are you settling in a state of heedlessness? Can you honestly identify a single, undivided intention in your heart at this moment? If your state is composite—part longing for Allah and part love of the world—then according to the logic of this passage, you are neither truly moving nor truly resting. What single step will you take today to make your condition a pure state of departure toward Him or a settled state of obedience?
?
The passage says that a state is called 'departure' only if it is preceded by a state in another place. Think about your actions yesterday: were they in a different place than your actions today? If you find yourself repeating the same mistake, then your 'state' is not departure from that sin but settling in it. Are you content with that? The definition forces you to evaluate whether your life has actual movement or just stagnation. If today's state is the same as yesterday's, then you are at rest—but is that the rest of contentment in obedience or the rest of laziness in sin? What will you change to make tomorrow's state a genuine departure?
?
The argument in this lesson hinges on the impossibility of two states existing in one condition. Yet how often do we try to live in two contradictory states—claiming trust in Allah while anxiously controlling outcomes, or declaring love for the Prophet while neglecting his sunnah? The passage challenges you to admit that such contradictions are impossible in reality; your heart can only be in one state at a time. Which state truly dominates your condition right now? Strip away the pretense and identify that single state. Then ask yourself: is it a state that will be pleasing to Allah when you meet Him?

Endurance as a Created Accident and Refutation
Definition of endurance as an accident and response to an objection about its creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Maturidi school defines endurance (baqa') as a created accident and how it responds to an objection against this view.

Core Idea
Endurance is a created accident, not a self-subsistent attribute.

The concept of endurance was debated among kalam schools. The Maturidis argue that endurance is a distinct accident created by Allah in bodies to enable their continued existence. The passage presents the definition and then addresses a potential objection: if endurance is created, why doesn't it always accompany the body? This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of endurance
Read the first part of the passage where the author defines endurance as a single accident (permanence and continuation). Note that it does not exist in the first moment but in the second.
This establishes the baseline: endurance is not the essence but a separate accident.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objection: if the body endures by a created endurance, why can't it exist without endurance for some moments? The author presents this to refute it.
3
Understand the response
The author argues that if it's impossible for a body to exist two moments without description of endurance, then it's impossible that the attribute causing endurance is not created. This is analogous to accidents always being created in bodies.
The key is the necessary connection between existence and the accident.
4
See the reversal
The author reverses the question: if the body were enduring by itself, it could exist two moments without endurance, but that's impossible. Thus the objection collapses.
This shows the internal consistency of the Maturidi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما في البقاء فهو اسم لعرض واحد وهو الدوام واستمرار الوجود، ولن يوجد الأول في الثاني ولا يكون باقيًا في الوقت الأول بل يكون باقيًا في الثاني. ومما سألوا نحو قولهم : إن الجسم لو كان باقيًا ببقاء يخلق له لجاز أن يكون موجودًا وقتين فأكثر، ولا يخلق له البقاء، ويخلق فيه من جنس العرض الذي كان موجودًا في حال الحدوث، ويوجد أوقاتاً غير باق؟ والجواب عنه أن يقال: إذا استحال وجوده وقتين لا يوصف بالبقاء استحال أن لا يخلق فيه المعنى الذي من الوصف له به كالجسم لما استحال تعريه عن الأعراض استحال وجوده وقتًا ولا يخلق فيه شيء منه. ثم يعكس عليهم هذا السؤال فيقال : لو كان الجسم باقيًا لنفسه أو لا لنفسه أو لا لمعنى لجاز أن يكون موجودًا وقتين غير موصوف بهذا الوصف، ولما استحال ذلك عند من نفى البقاء بطل ما أوردوه من السؤال.
As for endurance (baqa'), it is a name for a single accident (ard), which is permanence (dawam) and continuation of existence. And the first does not exist in the second, nor is it enduring in the first moment, but it is enduring in the second. And among what they asked is their saying: If the body were enduring by an endurance (baqa') created for it, then it would be possible for it to exist for two moments or more, and the endurance is not created for it, and there is created in it from the genus of the accident that existed in the state of origination (huduth), and it exists at times without enduring? The answer is that it is said: When it is impossible for it to exist for two moments without being described as endurance, it is impossible that the attribute by which it is described should not be created in it, just as when it is impossible for the body to be stripped of accidents, it is impossible for it to exist for a moment without something of them being created in it. Then they reverse this question and say: If the body were enduring by itself, or not by itself, or not by an attribute, then it would be possible for it to exist for two moments not described with this description. And since that is impossible according to the one who negates endurance, what they have put forward of the question is nullified.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage defines endurance (baqa') as the name for a single accident, namely permanence (dawam) and continuation of existence. The author clarifies that an entity does not endure in the first moment but in the second moment. The passage then presents an objection: If a body were enduring by an endurance created for it, it could exist for two or more moments; but if no endurance is created for it, then it would exist at times without enduring? The answer is that if it is impossible for something to exist for two moments without being described as endurance, it is impossible that the attribute (i.e., endurance) should not be created in it. This is analogous to a body: since it cannot exist stripped of accidents, it cannot exist for a moment without some accident being created in it. Then the author reverses the question: if the body were enduring by itself (without a created attribute), it could exist for two moments without being described as endurance. Since that is impossible for those who negate endurance (i.e., they cannot conceive of a body existing without becoming), the objection fails.

حدوث

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Endurance is a self-subsisting attribute of bodies, not a created accident.
Correction: The passage argues that endurance is a single accident created by Allah, and that without it, a body cannot persist for two moments.

Checkpoint

A) The essence of a thing
B) A self-subsisting attribute
C) A single accident
D) The absence of change

Key Takeaway
Endurance is a created accident that is necessary for a thing to persist beyond the first moment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson teaches that your very existence at each moment depends on a newly created accident of endurance from Allah. Have you ever truly felt this dependency in your daily life, or do you move through your day assuming that your existence is automatic? ?
If endurance is not inherent but must be granted anew every moment, then your ability to persist in good deeds also requires Allah's constant renewal. What good deed are you currently neglecting because you rely on your own strength rather than seeking Allah's continuous support? Will you ask Him right now to grant you the endurance to resume it?
?
The opponents thought that if endurance were created, a body could exist without it. This reveals a deeper assumption: that things can persist on their own. In what area of your faith have you been assuming that your 'endurance' is self-generated—perhaps in patience, in worship, or in avoiding a particular sin? Confess this to Allah and commit to recognizing your need for His created endurance each moment.

Refuting Objections on Divine Power Over the Body
Al-Nasafi answers two objections about the body as an object of power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Nasafi refutes the objection that a body enduring by an attribute cannot be an object of divine power.

Core Idea
Divine power creates attributes, not the already-existing self.

In kalam, the relationship between God's power and contingent objects is a central debate. The Mu'tazila raised an objection: if a body endures through an attribute (baqā') subsisting in it, then the body itself cannot be an object of divine power. Imam al-Nasafi provides a two-part response, clarifying that divine power pertains to the creation of attributes like motion and preservation, not to the body's own existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage: the objector claims that if a body endures by an attribute, then its self (nafs) is not an object of power for the Eternal.
The objector assumes that endurance (baqā') makes the body independent of divine power.
2
Understand al-Nasafi's first answer
He points out that denying endurance does not make the self an object of power with the attribute of endurance either. In fact, power does not relate to the existent according to the Mu'tazila.
This is a dialectical move: even if we accept their premise, the conclusion doesn't follow.
3
Analyze the second objection and response
The second objection uses an analogy: if a moving thing moves by a meaning, then the body would not be an object of power as moving. The objector then says power to move is power to create motion. Al-Nasafi counters that power to preserve is power to create preservation.
This shows consistency: if they accept the analogy, they must accept the same logic for preservation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما سألوا عنه قولهم: إن الجسم لو كان باقيًا يقوم به لم يكن نفسه مقدورًا للقديم. والجواب عنه أن نفي البقاء لم يجعل النفس مقدورًا للقديم بوصف البقاء أيضًا، ونزيد عليه بأن نقول: لا يكون مقدورًا له في حال الوجود، لأن القدرة عندهم لا تتعلق بالموجود. ثم يقال لهذا السائل: لو كان المتحرك متحركًا لمعنى لم يكن الجسم مقدورًا للقديم متحركًا؟ فإن قال: القدرة على تحريكه قدرة على خلق الحركة له. قيل له: والقدرة على إبقائه، قدرة على خلق بقائه.
And among what they asked about is their statement: If the body were enduring by something subsisting in it, then its very self would not be an object of power for the Eternal. The answer to it is that the negation of endurance did not make the self an object of power for the Eternal with the attribute of endurance either. And we add to it by saying: It is not an object of power for Him in the state of existence, because power, according to them, does not relate to the existent. Then it is said to this questioner: If the moving thing were moving by a meaning, then the body would not be an object of power for the Eternal as moving? If he says: The power to move it is a power to create motion for it. It is said to him: And the power to preserve it is a power to create its preservation.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer exchange. The objector argues that if a body endures through an attribute (baqā') subsisting in it, then the body itself cannot be an object of divine power because it already exists. Al-Nasafi answers by clarifying that denying endurance does not make the self an object of power with the attribute of endurance; rather, the body is not an object of power in the state of existence at all, since the Mu'tazila hold that power only affects non-existents. The second objection asks: if a moving thing is moving by a meaning, then the body cannot be an object of power as moving. The objector suggests that power to move is power to create motion. Al-Nasafi retorts that similarly, power to preserve is power to create preservation, showing consistency.

Key Terms
القديم
البقاء
القدرة
المقدور
الحركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if a body endures by an attribute, God cannot have power over it because the body already exists.
Correction: Al-Nasafi shows that divine power does not relate to the existent self but to the creation of attributes like motion and preservation.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nasafi's answer, why is the body not an object of divine power in the state of existence?
A) Because it is already enduring by itself.
B) Because divine power does not relate to the existent according to the Mu'tazila.
C) Because the body is not a possible entity.
D) Because power is an accident that cannot subsist in the body.

Key Takeaway
Divine power creates attributes like motion and preservation, not the already-existing self of a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your very existence is a constant creation by God, or do you take for granted that you simply 'are'? What would change if you saw every breath as a newly created act of His power?
?
If divine power creates your attributes—your endurance, your motion, your perseverance—are you acknowledging that He is actively sustaining every good quality you have? What does that imply for your pride in your own abilities?
?
Al-Nasafi argues that power to preserve is power to create preservation. When you pray for God to preserve you, do you truly believe He is creating that preservation anew each moment, or do you assume it is automatic? How can you cultivate a deeper awareness of His continuous creative act in your life starting today?

Mu'tazila Objection on One Time vs Many Times and Initial Response
Mu'tazila object with two distinctions, author begins reply focusing on the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's objection distinguishing one time from many times and how the author begins to reply by showing it depends on definitions of motion.

Core Idea
Objections often assume definitions that may not apply.

The Mu'tazila raise an objection about how a body can be devoid of motion at first but not later, and how a healthy person cannot act initially. The author starts his reply by noting that the first objection only applies to certain definitions of motion. This lesson unpacks the objection and the initial response, though the response is cut off mid-sentence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice that the text ends abruptly; the response is incomplete.
2
Identify the two distinctions
The Mu'tazila present two cases that distinguish between one time and many times. Find each one and note how they argue that something is possible in the first time but impossible later.
The first is about a body being devoid of motion and rest; the second about a healthy person's capability.
3
Observe the author's immediate classification
The author says the first objection only applies to some opponents. He then explains that for those who define motion as a single mode with a condition, the objection does not apply.
4
Understand the definitional argument
The author explains that according to one definition, motion is a mode in the second time conditioned by absence of a mode in another place. In the first moment, the body has a mode of the genus of motion but it is not called motion because the condition is lacking.
This shows the body is never truly devoid of something like motion.
5
Recognize the cut-off
The passage ends while the author is describing those who define motion as two modes. The full response is not given.
This lesson only covers the beginning of the reply.

فاعترضوا على هذا الكلام بفصلين في كل واحد منهما للتفرقة بين وقت واحد وبين أوقات كثيرة. أحدهما : أن الجسم يجوز خلوه عن الحركة والسكون بل يجب ذلك في أول أحوال وجوده ثم يستحيل ذلك في الوقت الثاني والثالث. والآخر أن الصحيح والسليم غير قادر على الفعل أول كونه ثم لم يجز أبدًا. والجواب: أن السؤال الأول يتوجه على بعض الخصوم دون البعض، ومن توجه عليه من حيث الظاهر فهو إما مغالطة منهم مع علمهم أنه غير لازم، وإما جهل بالحقائق. وبيان هذا أن من قال الحركة اسم لكون واحد وهو الكون الموجود في الزمان الثاني إلا أن شرط استحقاقه هذا الاسم بعدم كون في مكان آخر، والجسم في أول أحوال وجوده لا يخلو عن كون، وهو من جنس ما يسمى حركة إلا أنه لا يسمى به لفقد شرط وهو بعدم كون له في مكان آخر، فإذا لم يخل الجسم في أول أحوال الوجود عما هو من جنس الحركة فلم يكن هذا الاعتراض متوجها عليهم. وإنما يتوجه من حيث الظاهر على من يقول : إن الحركة اسم لكونين في مكانين والسكون اسم لكونين في مكان واحد، وشرط الكونين زمانان لأنهما فعلان فلا يوجدان إلا في زمانين، إذ في كل زمان يوجد فيه فصل واحد، والجسم في أول أحوال وجوده لا يخلو عن كون وهو فعل واحد، وهم ألزموا خلو القدرة عن
They objected to this statement with two distinctions in each of which there is a differentiation between one time and many times. One of them: that a body may be devoid of motion and rest, indeed it must be so in the first state of its existence, then that becomes impossible in the second and third times. And the other: that the healthy and sound person is not capable of the act at first of his existence, then he never becomes capable ever. The answer: that the first objection applies to some opponents not to others, and the one to whom it applies in appearance is either a sophistry from them with their knowledge that it is not necessary, or ignorance of realities. And the explanation of this is that he who says that motion is a name for a single mode which is the mode existing in the second time, except that the condition for its deserving this name is the non-existence of a mode in another place, and the body in the first state of its existence is not devoid of a mode, and it is of the genus of what is called motion except that it is not called by it due to the loss of a condition which is the non-existence of a mode in another place. So since the body is not devoid in the first state of existence of what is of the genus of motion, this objection is not directed against them. Rather it applies in appearance to those who say that motion is a name for two modes in two places and rest is a name for two modes in one place, and the condition for the two modes is two times because they are two acts so they exist only in two times, since in each time only one division exists, and the body in the first state of its existence is not devoid of a mode which is a single act, and they obligated the vacuity of power from...
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage records the Mu'tazilite objection and the beginning of the reply. The Mu'tazila distinguish between one time and many times with two examples: a body can be without motion at first but not later, and a healthy person cannot act at first but later can. The answer first states that the first objection only applies to some opponents (those who define motion as two modes). The author then explains that according to one definition, motion is a single mode in the second time with the condition of absence of a mode in another place. Thus at the first moment the body has a mode, but it is not called motion because it lacks that condition. So the body is never devoid of something like motion, so the objection fails. He then notes that the objection only applies to those who define motion as two modes in two places, and then the text cuts off.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first objection disproves the Maturidi position on motion.
Correction: The author shows that the objection depends on how motion is defined; for a definition that treats motion as a single mode with a condition, the objection does not apply.

Checkpoint
What is the first distinction the Mu'tazila make in their objection?
A) That a body can be devoid of motion and rest at first but not later.
B) That a healthy person is unable to act at first but becomes able.
C) That power persists over time.
D) That motion requires two modes.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila's first objection hinges on the definition of motion; the author shows that under one definition the objection does not apply because the body is never devoid of something like motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your definition of a key concept (e.g., 'faith', 'free will', 'success') is universal, only to discover that others define it differently? How did that affect your ability to engage with their arguments? This lesson shows the danger of unconscious definitional assumptions. Will you now make it a habit to clarify definitions before entering debate?
?
The author accuses some opponents of either sophistry or ignorance. When you find yourself disagreeing with someone on a core belief, do you first examine whether the terms are being used consistently? Or are you quick to dismiss their position? What would it cost you to humbly check definitions before pressing an objection?
?
If the definition of 'motion' can change the logical validity of an entire argument, what does that say about the importance of precise terminology in your own faith? Are there areas of your belief where you have not properly defined terms—and thus may be vulnerable to objections? What step will you take this week to strengthen your grasp of the definitions used in Islamic theology?
