Refuting Double Punishment for Omission and Commission
The author refutes double punishment for omission and commission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mu'tazilite claim that one deserves double punishment for omitting a commanded act and committing a prohibited act leads to unacceptable conclusions.

Core Idea
Double punishment for omission leads to absurd theological outcomes.

The author takes on a problematic Mu'tazilite position that distinguishes between punishment for committing a bad act and punishment for omitting a good act. He demonstrates that this logic, when applied consistently, yields results that contradict fundamental Islamic principles. In this passage, you will see how the author uses the examples of zina and prayer to expose the flaw.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then the English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent says a person who fails to do a commanded act and does a prohibited act deserves two punishments: one for the bad act, one for the omitted good. Note this.
3
Follow the refutation via zina
The author applies the same logic to zina: if one is commanded to leave zina, then committing zina would also mean omitting the good of leaving it, leading to double punishment. See the absurdity.
4
See the prayer example
The opponent says leaving prayer is not bad because it's not explicitly forbidden. Since everything permissible is good, leaving prayer becomes good. The author calls this a departure from religion.
This shows the internal inconsistency.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Note the author's strong condemnation: 'This is a departure from the religion.' Consider why this refutation is important for Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعالى، وإن كانوا يقولون ذلك بفعل العبد وكسبه وهو به عاص، فكان إكفار أولى إذ زعمت أن الله تعالى يعذب العبد لا على فعل محدث أو مكتسب. ثم قيل له: إن هذا المأمور لو لم يفعل المأمورية وتغير تغيرا قبيحا؟ قال: استحق قسطين من العذاب أحدهما للقبيح الذي فعله، والآخر للحسن الذي لم يفعله وهذا كله خروج عن أقاويل أهل الإسلام. ثم قيل له: إن المكلف لما نهى عن الزنا فقد أمر بتركه لا محالة، فينبغي أن يقال: إنه لما زنى يجب عليه حدان أحدهما للزنا الذي فعله، والآخر للحسن الذي لم يفعله وهو ترك الزنا. وكذا في جميع هذه الأفعال، فأما ترك هذه الأفعال فغير واجب عليه، ويلزم ألا يكون ترك الزنا وشرب الخمر واللواطة طاعة ويرتكب ذلك. أسباب الحدود، فزعم عنه توجه هذا الإلزام عليه أنه نهى عن. وكذا زعم أن ترك الصلاة إذا لم يكن منهيًا عنه ليس بقبيح، ثم عنده ما ليس بقبيح بل هو مباح يكون حسنًا إذ من مذهبه أن كل مباح حسن، وإنما يجب بحسن زائد فيكون على هذا ترك الصلاة حسنًا، وهذا خروج عن الدين والانسلاخ منه بمرة، نعوذ بالله من قول هذا عقباه.
Exalted is He, and if they say that by the action of the slave and his acquisition he is disobedient with it, then declaring disbelief is more appropriate, since you claimed that God punishes the slave not for a created or acquired act. Then it was said to him: If this commanded one did not do the commanded thing and changed in a bad manner? He said: He deserves two portions of punishment: one for the bad that he did, and the other for the good that he did not do. And all this is a departure from the statements of the people of Islam. Then it was said to him: When the responsible one is forbidden from zina, he is undoubtedly commanded to leave it. So it should be said: When he commits zina, two fixed punishments are obligatory upon him: one for the zina he committed, and the other for the good that he did not do, which is leaving zina. And likewise in all these acts. As for leaving these acts, it is not obligatory upon him, and it follows that leaving zina, drinking wine, and sodomy is not obedience, and he commits that. [And the causes of the limits? So he claimed about him that this entailment is directed upon him that he forbade?] And likewise he claimed that leaving prayer, if it is not forbidden, is not bad. Then according to him, what is not bad but is permissible is good, since it is his doctrine that every permissible is good. And it is obligatory due to an additional good. So on this basis, leaving prayer is good. And this is a departure from the religion and a complete shedding of it. We seek refuge with Allah from the outcome of this saying.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a Mu'tazilite position concerning double punishment. The author first presents the opponent's view: that a person who fails to perform a commanded act and instead performs a prohibited act deserves two separate punishments—one for the prohibited act committed and one for the good act omitted. The author declares this to be contrary to the views of mainstream Islam. He then applies the same logic to the case of zina: one who commits zina is also commanded to leave it, so according to the opponent he would deserve two fixed punishments (ḥadd). The author points out the absurdity that by this reasoning, leaving zina, drinking wine, and sodomy would not be considered acts of obedience, and leaving these acts is not obligatory upon him. Then the author mentions an unclear statement about 'causes of limits.' Finally, he addresses the issue of leaving prayer: the opponent claims that leaving prayer is not bad because it is not explicitly forbidden, and since everything permissible is good, leaving prayer becomes a good act. The author vehemently denounces this as a complete departure from religion.

طاعة
حدود
قسطين

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Omission of a commanded act and commission of a prohibited act each deserve separate punishments, resulting in double punishment.
Correction: The author shows that this logic leads to absurdities like making leaving prayer a good act, and it contradicts Islamic teachings that punishment is for the act itself.

Checkpoint
What does the author say is a consequence of the opponent's claim that one deserves double punishment for omitting a good act and committing a bad act?
A) It makes leaving prayer a good act.
B) It makes zina permissible.
C) It eliminates the need for punishment.
D) It affirms divine grace beyond merit.

Key Takeaway
Double punishment for omission is refuted because it leads to contradictions, such as labeling the omission of prayer as a good act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Have you ever rationalized a sin by thinking, 'At least I'm not doing something worse'? This lesson shows that neglecting obligations can itself be a serious departure from Islam. What is one obligation (e.g., charity, fasting, honesty) that you have been neglecting, and what specific step will you take today to fulfil it?
?
The Mu'tazilites relied on their own reasoning without submitting to revelation. In what area of your life do you find yourself trusting your own judgment over what Allah has commanded? How will you surrender that area to Allah starting now?

The Three Positions on Command and Its Opposite
Reports disagreement on command-opposite relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main positions on whether a command implies a prohibition of its opposite, and the difference between 'indication' and 'necessitation'.

Core Idea
The relationship between command and its opposite is debated: indication, necessitation, or no connection?

In this passage, Imam Nasafi reports the disagreement among scholars on the logical relationship between a command and its opposite. Some argue that a command directly indicates prohibition of the opposite, others say it necessitates it, and some are lenient with terminology. This sets the stage for the later refutation of a middle position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation to grasp the different opinions.
Pay attention to the terms 'دلالة' (indication) and 'اقتضاء' (necessitation).
2
Identify the three views
List the three positions mentioned: (1) command indicates prohibition of its opposite, (2) command necessitates prohibition of its opposite, (3) some use terms loosely without distinction.
3
Notice the loose usage
The third view treats 'indication' and 'necessitation' as interchangeable. Why might this be problematic? Reflect on precision in theological terminology.
This loose usage sets up the later critique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفت عباراتهم في ذلك فزعم بعضهم : أن الأمر بالشيء يدل على النهي عن ضده، والنهي عن الشيء يدل على الأمر بضده. وقال بعضهم: الأمر بالشيء يقتضي نهيًا عن ضده وكذا على القلب. ومنهم من يطلق ما يتفق عليه من اللفظ ولا يفرق بين لفظة الدلالة ولفظة الاقتضاء على طريق التساهل. فلما تحير هذا الرجل فى هذه المسألة وتجاهل، وزعم أن ترك الزنا ليس بمأمور به وترك الصلاة ليس بمنهي عنه، زعم أن الأمر بالشيء ليس بنهي عن ضده، ولا يدل عليه ولا يقتضيه، ولا حكم له في الضد، ويبقى الضد على ما كان قبل ورود الأمر، وكذا في جانب النهي. وزعم أن ثواب من انعدم منه الزنا لا بمقابلة فعل ضده، وعقاب من انعدمت منه الصلاة لا بمقابلة فعل ضده. ثم إن بعض المتكلمين ممن تكلم في أصول الفقه من أهل ديارنا ذكر أني أقول : إن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده لا نهيه و لا أقول إنه نهى عن ضده ولا أقول إنه يدل على نهيه ولا أنه يقتضي نهيًا. ولست أدري ماذا كان رأيه في الكلام؟ أنه هو المعنى القائم بالذات المنافي للسكوت والآفة وهو بعينه أمر بما أمر به نهي عما نهى عنه كما هو مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة. أو هو هذه الحروف المنظومة والأصوات المقطوعة ، وما كان أمرًا لا يكون نهيًا، وما كان نهيًا لا يكون أمرًا كما هو مذهب المعتزلة ؟ وكذا ما كان رأيه أن توجه الوعيد على تارك الصلاة لارتكابه ضدها المنهي عنه وهو الترك الذي هو فعل كما هو مذهب جميع أهل القبلة. أما لانعدام ما أمر به من غير فعل ارتكبه كما هو مذهب هاشم. فإن كان متمسكا بمذاهب أهل السنة والجماعة فقال ما قال لرأي منح له غير معروض على قوانين الأصول، ولا مبني على قواعد المذهب، وإن كان مائلاً إلى مذهب القوم في مسألة الكلام وإلى مذهب أبي هاشم في هذه المسألة فقال ما قال بناء على رأيه، غير أنه مع هذا خالف أبا هاشم حيث جعل الأمر حكمًا في ضد المأمور به وهو الكراهية، وأبو هاشم أبى هذا. وهذا من أعجب الرأي؛ فإنه إن كان الوعيد متوجهًا على من انعدم المأمور من قبله وعوقب لذلك كما هو مذهب أبي هاشم، فأي حاجة إلى إثبات الكراهية في الضد، والوعيد بدونه متوجه والعقاب متحقق، وإن لم يكن بد من فعل محظور يرتكبه ووزر عظيم لحقه لتوجه الوعيد وتحقق العقوبة، وذلك هو فعل الترك، فكيف يزعم بتوجهه كل الوعيد الوارد لتارك الفرائض وثبوت العقوبة له لو لم يتغمده الله برحمته بمباشرة فعل مكروه ليس بمنهى عنه ولا محظور، وهذا مما يأباه جميع أهل القبلة.
And their statements differed regarding that: some claimed that commanding something indicates prohibition of its opposite, and prohibiting something indicates command of its opposite. And some said: commanding something necessitates a prohibition of its opposite, and likewise conversely. And among them are those who use the agreed-upon expression loosely and do not distinguish between the word 'indication' and the word 'necessitation' by way of leniency. When this man became confused in this issue and feigned ignorance, and claimed that abandoning adultery is not commanded and abandoning prayer is not prohibited, he claimed that commanding something is not a prohibition of its opposite, nor does it indicate it nor necessitate it, nor does it have any ruling concerning the opposite, and the opposite remains as it was before the command came, and likewise on the side of prohibition. And he claimed that the reward of one from whom adultery is absent is not in exchange for the act of its opposite, and the punishment of one from whom prayer is absent is not in exchange for the act of its opposite. Then one of the mutakallimun among those who spoke on the principles of jurisprudence from the people of our region mentioned that I say: commanding something necessitates dislike (karaha) of its opposite, not its prohibition, and I do not say that it is a prohibition of its opposite, nor do I say that it indicates its prohibition, nor that it necessitates a prohibition. And I do not know what his opinion was regarding speech: that it is the meaning subsisting in the essence which is contrary to silence and defect, and it is itself a command for what it commands and a prohibition for what it prohibits, as is the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. Or is it these arranged letters and segmented sounds, and whatever is a command is not a prohibition, and whatever is a prohibition is not a command, as is the madhhab of the Mu'tazila? And likewise what was his opinion that the threat (wa'id) is directed upon the one who leaves prayer for committing its opposite which is prohibited, namely the abandonment which is an act, as is the madhhab of all Ahl al-Qibla. Or because of the absence of what was commanded without committing an act, as is the madhhab of Hashim. If he adhered to the madhhabs of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a and said what he said based on an opinion granted to him not subjected to the principles of usul, nor built on the foundations of the madhhab; and if he inclined toward the madhhab of the people in the issue of speech and toward the madhhab of Abu Hashim in this issue, then he said what he said based on his opinion, except that along with this he opposed Abu Hashim in that he made the command have a ruling concerning the opposite of the commanded thing, namely dislike (karaha), while Abu Hashim rejected this. And this is among the most astonishing opinions: for if the threat is directed upon one from whom the commanded act is absent and he is punished for that as is the madhhab of Abu Hashim, then what need is there to establish dislike (karaha) in the opposite, while the threat is directed without it and the punishment is actualized? And if there must be a prohibited act he commits and a great burden upon him for the threat to be directed and the punishment to be realized, that is the act of abandonment. So how can he claim that all the threat reported for the one who leaves obligatory acts is directed and the punishment is established for him—if God does not envelop him in His mercy—through performing a disliked act that is neither prohibited nor forbidden? And this is rejected by all Ahl al-Qibla.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a particular position on the relationship between command (amr) and prohibition (nahy) regarding opposites. The author first reports that there is disagreement: some say a command indicates prohibition of its opposite; others say it necessitates prohibition of the opposite; and some are lax and don't distinguish between 'indication' and 'necessitation'. Then a certain person (Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i) is mentioned who claims that leaving a commanded act (like prayer) is not itself prohibited, and that a command does not imply anything about its opposite. Another mutakallim from the author's region says that commanding necessitates only dislike (karaha) of the opposite, not prohibition. The author then questions this mutakallim's view on the nature of divine speech: if he is a Sunni (attributes of meaning), then his own statement would mean that the same attribute commands and prohibits simultaneously—but in this view, command and prohibition are the same attribute, so why does he assert a difference? If he is a Mu'tazili (created speech), then command and prohibition are distinct, so his claim that command implies dislike rather than prohibition would be consistent with Mu'tazili principles. The author then examines the inconsistency regarding the threat (wa'id) for leaving prayer. According to Ahl al-Qibla, the threat is for committing the prohibited act of abandonment (which is an act). According to Abu Hashim, the threat is for the absence of the commanded act without any intervening act. The mutakallim in question claims that the threat is directed but the opposite acts are only disliked, not prohibited. The author finds this contradictory: if threat is for absence (as per Abu Hashim), dislike is unnecessary; if threat requires a prohibited act, then that act is abandonment itself, which would be prohibited—but the mutakallim says it is only disliked. Thus, the position is rejected by all Ahl al-Qibla.

الأمر
النهي
الضد
الدلالة
الاقتضاء

Key Takeaway
The relationship between a command and its opposite is subject to scholarly disagreement, with some seeing a direct implication and others denying any necessary connection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refuting the Middle Position: Dislike vs. Prohibition
Introduces and refutes the view that command necessitates dislike.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the middle position that command necessitates only dislike (karaha) of its opposite, and the author's refutation based on inconsistency regarding the divine threat.

Core Idea
Claiming command implies dislike, not prohibition, leads to contradictions in theodicy.

After presenting the main views, the author introduces a middle position: that command necessitates only dislike (karaha) of its opposite, not prohibition. He then criticizes this view by questioning its implications for divine speech and punishment, showing it leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the middle position
Read the part where a mutakallim claims that commanding something necessitates dislike (karaha) of its opposite, not prohibition.
Note that this is a compromise between the two extremes.
2
Understand the author's questioning
The author questions the mutakallim's view on divine speech (kalam). Is it an attribute of meaning (Sunni view) or created letters (Mu'tazili view)? This affects whether command and prohibition can be distinct.
3
Analyze the threat (wa'id) inconsistency
The author argues that if the threat for leaving prayer is due to absence of the act, then dislike is unnecessary; if it requires a prohibited act, then abandonment must be prohibited, not merely disliked.
This is the core of the refutation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that this middle position is rejected by all Ahl al-Qibla because it conflicts with established doctrines of reward and punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلفت عباراتهم في ذلك فزعم بعضهم : أن الأمر بالشيء يدل على النهي عن ضده، والنهي عن الشيء يدل على الأمر بضده. وقال بعضهم: الأمر بالشيء يقتضي نهيًا عن ضده وكذا على القلب. ومنهم من يطلق ما يتفق عليه من اللفظ ولا يفرق بين لفظة الدلالة ولفظة الاقتضاء على طريق التساهل. فلما تحير هذا الرجل فى هذه المسألة وتجاهل، وزعم أن ترك الزنا ليس بمأمور به وترك الصلاة ليس بمنهي عنه، زعم أن الأمر بالشيء ليس بنهي عن ضده، ولا يدل عليه ولا يقتضيه، ولا حكم له في الضد، ويبقى الضد على ما كان قبل ورود الأمر، وكذا في جانب النهي. وزعم أن ثواب من انعدم منه الزنا لا بمقابلة فعل ضده، وعقاب من انعدمت منه الصلاة لا بمقابلة فعل ضده. ثم إن بعض المتكلمين ممن تكلم في أصول الفقه من أهل ديارنا ذكر أني أقول : إن الأمر بالشيء يقتضي كراهة ضده لا نهيه و لا أقول إنه نهى عن ضده ولا أقول إنه يدل على نهيه ولا أنه يقتضي نهيًا. ولست أدري ماذا كان رأيه في الكلام؟ أنه هو المعنى القائم بالذات المنافي للسكوت والآفة وهو بعينه أمر بما أمر به نهي عما نهى عنه كما هو مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة. أو هو هذه الحروف المنظومة والأصوات المقطوعة ، وما كان أمرًا لا يكون نهيًا، وما كان نهيًا لا يكون أمرًا كما هو مذهب المعتزلة ؟ وكذا ما كان رأيه أن توجه الوعيد على تارك الصلاة لارتكابه ضدها المنهي عنه وهو الترك الذي هو فعل كما هو مذهب جميع أهل القبلة. أما لانعدام ما أمر به من غير فعل ارتكبه كما هو مذهب هاشم. فإن كان متمسكا بمذاهب أهل السنة والجماعة فقال ما قال لرأي منح له غير معروض على قوانين الأصول، ولا مبني على قواعد المذهب، وإن كان مائلاً إلى مذهب القوم في مسألة الكلام وإلى مذهب أبي هاشم في هذه المسألة فقال ما قال بناء على رأيه، غير أنه مع هذا خالف أبا هاشم حيث جعل الأمر حكمًا في ضد المأمور به وهو الكراهية، وأبو هاشم أبى هذا. وهذا من أعجب الرأي؛ فإنه إن كان الوعيد متوجهًا على من انعدم المأمور من قبله وعوقب لذلك كما هو مذهب أبي هاشم، فأي حاجة إلى إثبات الكراهية في الضد، والوعيد بدونه متوجه والعقاب متحقق، وإن لم يكن بد من فعل محظور يرتكبه ووزر عظيم لحقه لتوجه الوعيد وتحقق العقوبة، وذلك هو فعل الترك، فكيف يزعم بتوجهه كل الوعيد الوارد لتارك الفرائض وثبوت العقوبة له لو لم يتغمده الله برحمته بمباشرة فعل مكروه ليس بمنهى عنه ولا محظور، وهذا مما يأباه جميع أهل القبلة.
And their statements differed regarding that: some claimed that commanding something indicates prohibition of its opposite, and prohibiting something indicates command of its opposite. And some said: commanding something necessitates a prohibition of its opposite, and likewise conversely. And among them are those who use the agreed-upon expression loosely and do not distinguish between the word 'indication' and the word 'necessitation' by way of leniency. When this man became confused in this issue and feigned ignorance, and claimed that abandoning adultery is not commanded and abandoning prayer is not prohibited, he claimed that commanding something is not a prohibition of its opposite, nor does it indicate it nor necessitate it, nor does it have any ruling concerning the opposite, and the opposite remains as it was before the command came, and likewise on the side of prohibition. And he claimed that the reward of one from whom adultery is absent is not in exchange for the act of its opposite, and the punishment of one from whom prayer is absent is not in exchange for the act of its opposite. Then one of the mutakallimun among those who spoke on the principles of jurisprudence from the people of our region mentioned that I say: commanding something necessitates dislike (karaha) of its opposite, not its prohibition, and I do not say that it is a prohibition of its opposite, nor do I say that it indicates its prohibition, nor that it necessitates a prohibition. And I do not know what his opinion was regarding speech: that it is the meaning subsisting in the essence which is contrary to silence and defect, and it is itself a command for what it commands and a prohibition for what it prohibits, as is the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. Or is it these arranged letters and segmented sounds, and whatever is a command is not a prohibition, and whatever is a prohibition is not a command, as is the madhhab of the Mu'tazila? And likewise what was his opinion that the threat (wa'id) is directed upon the one who leaves prayer for committing its opposite which is prohibited, namely the abandonment which is an act, as is the madhhab of all Ahl al-Qibla. Or because of the absence of what was commanded without committing an act, as is the madhhab of Hashim. If he adhered to the madhhabs of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a and said what he said based on an opinion granted to him not subjected to the principles of usul, nor built on the foundations of the madhhab; and if he inclined toward the madhhab of the people in the issue of speech and toward the madhhab of Abu Hashim in this issue, then he said what he said based on his opinion, except that along with this he opposed Abu Hashim in that he made the command have a ruling concerning the opposite of the commanded thing, namely dislike (karaha), while Abu Hashim rejected this. And this is among the most astonishing opinions: for if the threat is directed upon one from whom the commanded act is absent and he is punished for that as is the madhhab of Abu Hashim, then what need is there to establish dislike (karaha) in the opposite, while the threat is directed without it and the punishment is actualized? And if there must be a prohibited act he commits and a great burden upon him for the threat to be directed and the punishment to be realized, that is the act of abandonment. So how can he claim that all the threat reported for the one who leaves obligatory acts is directed and the punishment is established for him—if God does not envelop him in His mercy—through performing a disliked act that is neither prohibited nor forbidden? And this is rejected by all Ahl al-Qibla.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a particular position on the relationship between command (amr) and prohibition (nahy) regarding opposites. The author first reports that there is disagreement: some say a command indicates prohibition of its opposite; others say it necessitates prohibition of the opposite; and some are lax and don't distinguish between 'indication' and 'necessitation'. Then a certain person (Abu Hashim al-Jubba'i) is mentioned who claims that leaving a commanded act (like prayer) is not itself prohibited, and that a command does not imply anything about its opposite. Another mutakallim from the author's region says that commanding necessitates only dislike (karaha) of the opposite, not prohibition. The author then questions this mutakallim's view on the nature of divine speech: if he is a Sunni (attributes of meaning), then his own statement would mean that the same attribute commands and prohibits simultaneously—but in this view, command and prohibition are the same attribute, so why does he assert a difference? If he is a Mu'tazili (created speech), then command and prohibition are distinct, so his claim that command implies dislike rather than prohibition would be consistent with Mu'tazili principles. The author then examines the inconsistency regarding the threat (wa'id) for leaving prayer. According to Ahl al-Qibla, the threat is for committing the prohibited act of abandonment (which is an act). According to Abu Hashim, the threat is for the absence of the commanded act without any intervening act. The mutakallim in question claims that the threat is directed but the opposite acts are only disliked, not prohibited. The author finds this contradictory: if threat is for absence (as per Abu Hashim), dislike is unnecessary; if threat requires a prohibited act, then that act is abandonment itself, which would be prohibited—but the mutakallim says it is only disliked. Thus, the position is rejected by all Ahl al-Qibla.

Key Terms
الأمر
النهي
الضد
الكراهية
الوعيد
توجه الوعيد
الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That command implies only dislike (karaha) of its opposite, not prohibition.
Correction: This view leads to inconsistency: if the threat for leaving prayer is for the absence of the act, dislike is unnecessary; if it is for an act, that act must be prohibited, not merely disliked.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the main problem with the view that command necessitates only dislike of its opposite?
A) It contradicts the Qur'an and Sunnah.
B) It is inconsistent because if the threat for leaving prayer is for absence, dislike is unnecessary; if for an act, that act must be prohibited, not disliked.
C) It is a Mu'tazili position and therefore rejected.
D) It has no logical basis.

Key Takeaway
The view that command implies only dislike of its opposite is inconsistent with the doctrine of divine punishment, as shown by Imam Nasafi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own understanding of God's commands, are you willing to accept that if an act is commanded, its opposite must be prohibited? How does this affect your daily choices about what you avoid?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Islam that, upon deeper reflection, contradicted another core belief you held? What did you do—ignore the contradiction or resolve it? This lesson shows that even great scholars struggled with consistency. What is your own standard for coherence in your faith?
?
The mutakallim in this passage held a view that seemed to reconcile positions but led to a logical dead end. Are there areas in your own life where you try to hold two contradictory ideas to avoid discomfort? What would it cost you to let go of one and embrace the truth clearly?

Three Proofs That Capability Is Fit for Opposites
Nasafi enumerates three arguments for capability being fit for opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational proofs used by Maturidi scholars to establish that human capability (qudra) is fit for performing opposite actions.

Core Idea
Capability is fit for opposites, otherwise acts would be compelled.

In this passage, Imam Nasafi defends the Maturidi position that human power (qudra) is not restricted to a single action but is inherently fit for opposites—such as belief or unbelief, movement or stillness. He offers three concise arguments drawn from everyday experience and logical necessity. As you read, notice how he moves from observable instruments to the implications of divine command to the distinction between natural and voluntary acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing to analyze. Then read the English translation to get the overall flow of the three proofs.
Notice the three 'منها' (one is...) markers.
2
Examine the first proof from instruments
The first proof argues that instruments like the tongue and hand are fit for opposite uses, so the power that employs them must also be fit for opposites.
This is an inductive argument from common experience.
3
Examine the second and third proofs
The second proof uses the command to the unbeliever to believe, which would be unbearable if he lacked power for belief. The third proof distinguishes natural acts (e.g., fire heating) from voluntary acts, showing that if power were not fit for opposites, all acts would be compelled.
The third proof includes the key distinction between the compelled and the capable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنا لما سبق منا القول في كيفية الخلاف بين القائلين أن الاستطاعة الفعل في كون القدرة صالحة للضدين فلابد من أن نبين ها هنا لبعض ما يتعلق به كل فريق على طريقة الاختصار ليقف عليه قارئ كتابنا هذا فنقول : إن القائلين بأنها تصلح للضدين يتعلقون بأشياء منها أن كل سبب من أسباب الفعل يصلح للضدين، فإن الآلات والأدوات المعدة لتتسم القدرة الناقصة صالحة للضدين كاللسان صلح للصدق والكذب والإقرار والتكذيب وغير ذلك، وكذا اليد تصلح لقتل الكفار والجهاد معهم، ولسفك دماء المسلمين والسعي في الأرض بالفساد وإثارة العبث والفتنة في العباد وكذا كل شيء. ومنها أن القدرة لو كانت لا تصلح للضدين لكان فيه تكليف ما لا يطاق (على ما بينا أن الكافر مأمور بالإيمان ولو لم يكن معه القدرة الصالحة للإيمان لكان فيه تكليف بما لا يطاق). ومنها أن كل ما يحصل به شيء ولا يصلح لضده يكون الحاصل به بالطبع لا بالاختيار كالثلج الذي يحصل به التبريد دون التسخين والنار التي يحصل بها التسخين دون التبريد، فيكون القول بأنها لا تصلح للضدين قولاً بالاضطرار وتقرير هذا أن قدرة الكفر التي وجدت في الكافر لو كانت غير صالحة للإيمان ولا تصلح إلا للكفر لا يتمكن الكافر المختص بقدرة الكفر أن ينفك عن قدرة الكفر، وأن يعدل عنه وأن يأتي بالإيمان، وكذا في الجالس مع القيام والمتحرك مع السكون وكل متضادين، وفيه أمران أحدهما : تكليف ما ليس في الوسع، والآخر: ارتفاع الفرق بين المضطر والقادر، والتمييز بين الأفعال الاختيارية والأفعال الاضطرارية، والفرق بينهما معلوم بطريق الاضطرار. فما أدى إلى خلافه كان بطلانه معلومًا بطريق الاضطرار.
Then, since we have already discussed the nature of the disagreement between those who say that capability is for the act in that power is fit for opposites, we must explain here some of what each group holds, in a concise manner, so that the reader of this our book may understand it. So we say: Those who say that it is fit for opposites adduce things: one is that every cause of an act is fit for opposites. For instruments and tools prepared for the incomplete power are fit for opposites: the tongue is fit for truth and falsehood, affirmation and denial, and so on. Likewise the hand is fit for killing the disbelievers and fighting them, and for shedding the blood of Muslims and causing corruption on earth, stirring up mischief and discord among people, and so on for everything. Another is that if power were not fit for opposites, there would be a command of what is unbearable (as we have explained: the unbeliever is commanded to believe, and if he did not have the power fit for belief, there would be a command of what is unbearable). Another is that everything by which something is obtained and which is not fit for its opposite, what is obtained by it is by nature, not by choice—like snow, which produces cooling without heating, and fire, which produces heating without cooling. So the statement that it is not fit for opposites is a statement of compulsion. The explanation of this: if the power for unbelief that exists in the unbeliever were not fit for belief and were only fit for unbelief, then the unbeliever, who is specified by the power for unbelief, would not be able to free himself from the power for unbelief, nor to turn away from it, nor to bring about belief; similarly for the sitting person with standing, the moving with rest, and every pair of opposites. In it are two matters: one is the command of what is not within capacity; the other is the removal of the distinction between the compelled and the capable, and the difference between voluntary and compulsory acts, while the difference between them is known instinctively. So whatever leads to the contrary of this, its falsity is known instinctively.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of three proofs (adilla) for the position that capability (qudra) is fit for opposites (ṣāliḥ li l-ḍiddayn). After a brief introductory note that he will explain concisely, Nasafi lists the proofs:

1. The proof from instruments: All causes of acts (like the tongue, hand) are fit for opposite actions (e.g., truth/falsehood, killing believers/disbelievers). If the power that uses these instruments were not fit for opposites, the instruments would not be so, but they are. Therefore power is fit for opposites.

2. The proof from the command of what is unbearable: If power were not fit for opposites, the unbeliever would not have power for faith, and commanding him to believe would be a command of what is unbearable. Since the command is valid (God does not command the impossible), power must be fit for both belief and unbelief.

3. The proof from natural vs. voluntary acts: Things that produce only one effect (like snow cooling, fire heating) do so by nature, not choice. If power were not fit for opposites, acts would be compelled, not voluntary. This would remove the instinctively known distinction between the compelled (muḍṭarr) and the capable (qādir), and between voluntary and compulsory actions. Such an implication is false, so the premise that power is not fit for opposites is false.

Key Terms
الضدين
سبب الفعل
الآلات
بالطبع
بالاختيار
الاضطرار
المضطر
القادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proofs for capability being fit for opposites are merely analogical and not logically necessary.
Correction: Nasafi's proofs are grounded in observable causation and logical necessity. The third proof in particular shows that denying the premise leads to a contradiction with instinctive knowledge, establishing a necessary truth.

Checkpoint
Which proof uses the example of snow and fire to argue that capability is fit for opposites?
A) The first proof from instruments.
B) The second proof from taklif ma la yutaq.
C) The third proof from natural vs. voluntary acts.
D) None of the above.

Key Takeaway
Capability is inherently fit for opposites; otherwise human actions would be compelled and divine commands could be unbearable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own tongue and hands, do you truly acknowledge that they are equally fit for truth and falsehood, for good and evil? How does this reality confront the excuses you make for your sins today?
?
This lesson proves that if your power were not fit for opposites, you would be no different from a fire that only burns or snow that only cools. Are there areas in your life where you live as if you are compelled—blaming your nature, your past, or your circumstances for your disobedience? What would it mean to fully accept that you are capable of obedience in that very moment?
?
The third proof says the difference between a voluntary act and a compelled act is known instinctively. Think of one specific sin you committed today. Did you feel, even for a moment, that you could have chosen otherwise? How will you use that freedom to turn back to Allah before you meet Him?

The Maturidi Response to the Mu'tazili Objection on Capability and Obligation
Imam Nasafi responds to the Mu'tazili objection by distinguishing between actual capability and the sound means to acquire it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi response to the Mu'tazili claim that obligating faith on a disbeliever is like obligating an action on a paralyzed person.

Core Idea
Capability for faith is based on sound means, not current possession.

Imam al-Nasafi tackles a subtle Mu'tazili objection: if a disbeliever has the capability for disbelief, and capability cannot serve opposites, how can faith be obligated? The objection uses an analogy to paralysis. Nasafi's response reveals a key distinction between actual capability and the potential capability through sound means.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the Mu'tazili analogy: a paralyzed hand cannot strike, so obligating striking is impossible; similarly, a disbeliever lacks faith capability, so obligating faith is impossible.
The objection hinges on the idea that capability cannot be for opposites.
2
Identify the three preventers in disbelief
Nasafi points out three things that prevent faith: the capability for disbelief, the will of disbelief, and disbelief itself.
These are all chosen by the person.
3
Compare the two cases
In paralysis, the preventer is involuntary; in disbelief, the preventer is self-inflicted. The paralyzed person is excused, the disbeliever is not.
The disbeliever had sound means but wasted them.
4
Reflect on the principle
Obligation is based on having sound means to perform an action at the time of command, not on current possession of capability.
This is the core Maturidi principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يجاب عن تمويه محققيهم : أن القدرة لما لم تصلح للضدين، وقدرة الكفر لا يتصور حصول الإيمان بها، فكان قيام قدرة الكفر وقيام نفس الكفر ووجود إرادة الكفر من هذا القادر مانعة من وجود قدرة الإيمان كوجود الزمانة والشلل في اليد يكون مانعا من وجود قدرة البطش. ثم التكليف مع الشلل تكليف ما لا يطاق فكذا هذا بل أولى، لأن المانع هناك ثلاثة أشياء: وهي قدرة الكفر وإرادته ونفسه. فإنا نقول : بذلك المانع كان ممنوع القدرة ، وبهذه المعاني كان مضيعًا القدرة، فكان ذلك معذوراً ولم يكلف، وبقي هذا مكلفا ولم يقدر على ما ذكرت في المثال.
And with this, the confusion of their investigators is answered: that capability (al-qudra) when it is not suitable for opposites, and the capability of disbelief cannot be imagined to have faith by it, so the presence of the capability of disbelief and the presence of disbelief itself and the existence of the will of disbelief from this capable person prevent the existence of the capability of faith just as the existence of palsy and paralysis in the hand prevents the existence of the capability of striking. Then obligation with paralysis is an obligation of what cannot be borne, so likewise this, rather more so, because the preventer there is three things: the capability of disbelief, its will, and its essence. So we say: By that preventer, he was prevented from capability, and by these meanings he was wasteful of capability, so that person was excused and not obligated, while this one remained obligated and did not have power over what was mentioned in the example.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The Mu'tazila argue that since the capability for disbelief cannot be used for faith, and the person already has the capability for disbelief along with the act and will of disbelief, this prevents the capability for faith. They analogize to a hand with palsy, which cannot strike, so obligating striking with a palsied hand is an obligation of what cannot be borne. Therefore, they claim, obligating faith on a disbeliever is likewise impossible. Imam al-Nasafi responds by distinguishing the two cases: in the case of palsy, the person is excused because the preventer (palsy) is beyond control, and they are not obligated. But in the case of disbelief, the person himself caused the loss of capability through his own choice (by persisting in disbelief), so he remains obligated even though he currently lacks the capability for faith. The difference is that the disbeliever had the sound means to have faith but wasted them, whereas the paralyzed person has no such means. Thus the obligation is valid because it is based on the soundness of means, not on actual capability.

Key Terms
قدرة (al-qudra)
تكليف (al-taklif)
إيمان (al-iman)
كفر (al-kufr)
شلل (al-shalal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Maturidi position obligates the impossible, ignoring the distinction between actual capability and potential capability through sound means.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that obligation is based on soundness of means at the time of the command, not on currently possessed capability. The disbeliever had sound means and wasted them, so he remains obligated.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidi response, why is the disbeliever obligated for faith despite lacking the capability for it?
A) Because he had the sound means to have faith but wasted them.
B) Because God can obligate anything regardless of capability.
C) Because the disbeliever actually has the capability for faith.
D) Because the obligation is based on the act of will, not capability.

Key Takeaway
The Maturidi view holds that obligation is based on having sound means to perform an action, not on currently possessing the capability; thus a disbeliever is obligated for faith despite lacking the capability because he had the means and wasted them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered that the 'sound means' Allah has given you—your intellect, health, time, and opportunities—are a trust that you can either use for faith or waste? Which of those means have you neglected or used for disobedience? What specific step will you take today to turn one of those means toward what pleases Allah?
?
The disbeliever in this passage is obligated despite lacking capability because he had the means and wasted them. What 'means' for increasing your faith do you possess but have not acted upon? Is there a verse you have not memorized, a prayer you delay, or a duty you avoid? Will you stop treating that as optional and start treating it as a debt you owe to your Creator?
?
This lesson forces us to examine the difference between an excuse we cannot help—like illness—and an excuse we create ourselves through negligence or sin. Where in your life have you used a self-inflicted weakness as an excuse to avoid obedience? What would it mean to repent from that excuse and reclaim the capability Allah originally gave you?

Division of Words into General and Homonymous
Nasafi classifies words covering a collection into general and homonymous, explaining their distinct rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the difference between general (عام) and homonymous (مشترك) words, and how each functions in Islamic legal discourse.

Core Idea
General words cover all individuals with same definition; homonyms cover multiple distinct meanings.

In this passage, Imām al-Nasafī introduces a crucial linguistic distinction for uṣūl al-fiqh: not all words that refer to a collection are general. Some words, like 'eye', have multiple distinct meanings and require evidence to specify which is intended. This classification helps avoid fallacious arguments from ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully, noting the two categories presented.
The passage begins with a sharp tone against opponents.
2
Identify the two categories
Look for the two divisions: general (عام) and homonymous (مشترك). Note how each is defined.
3
Understand the rule for each
For general terms, language requires inclusion of all individuals. For homonyms, inclusion depends on external evidence.
The passage emphasizes that homonyms have no generality by consensus.
4
Contrast the two
Notice the difference in how exclusion works: for general terms, exclusion is specification; for homonyms, it is not.
This is key for legal interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نكشف عن حقيقة هذا الكلام ليظهر غباوة القوم، وجهلهم بمواقع الخطاب مع دعاويهم العريضة فنقول :
إن اللفظ المستولى على المسميات ينقسم إلى قسمين:
أحدهما ما ينتظم جمعا كانت أفراده متفقة الحدود كقولنا : الرجال، فإن كل شخص يوافق غيره من الأشخاص الداخلة تحت هذا اللفظ في حد الرجولية وحد الرجل، وهذا هو العام المقتضي دخوله كل فرد من أفراده تحته تقتضيه اللغة، وخروج البعض منها عن حكم اللفظ يوجب خصوصا في الخطاب وسلبًا لعمومه.
والآخر : ما يتناول جمعا كانت أفراده مختلفة الحدود كلفظة العين، فإنها وإن كانت تقع على الآلة الباصرة وينبوع الماء وقرص الشمس وغير ذلك، فإنه لا شك أن حدودها مختلفة وأنواعها متغايرة، وما كان هذا سبيله لا يقتضي الخطاب الوارد بمثل هذه اللفظة الاشتغال على كل ما يصح دخوله تحت هذه اللفظة بل المراد هو الواحد منها غير عين، ويتوقف تعينه على وجود الدليل، ويسمى هذا النوع مشتركا، ولا عموم له بإجماع أهل الأصول، وعند تعين البعض مرادًا يخرج ما وراءه عن حكم الخطاب ولا يعد ذلك تخصيصا، وحاصل الفرق أن المشترك تثبت المساواة بين الجمع في جواز دخول كل من ذلك تحت اللفظ عند انتفاء دخول
Then we uncover the reality of this statement to show the stupidity of the people and their ignorance of the contexts of discourse, despite their broad claims. We say: The word that covers the named things (al-musammayāt) is divided into two parts: One of them is what encompasses a collection whose individuals have agreeing definitions, like our saying 'men' (al-rijāl), for every person agrees with other persons falling under this word in the definition of masculinity and the definition of man. This is the general (al-‘āmm) which necessitates the inclusion of every individual of its individuals under it, as language requires, and the exclusion of some of them from the ruling of the word necessitates particularity (khuṣūṣ) in the discourse and removal of its generality. The other is what includes a collection whose individuals have different definitions, like the word 'eye' (al-‘ayn), for although it applies to the seeing organ, the spring of water, the disk of the sun, and others, there is no doubt that their definitions differ and their kinds are diverse. That which is like this does not necessitate that the discourse occurring by such a word occupies itself with everything that may validly fall under this word; rather, the intended is one of them other than the other, and its specification depends on the existence of evidence. This type is called common (mushtarak), and it has no generality by consensus of the scholars of uṣūl. When some of them are specified as intended, what is beyond it exits from the ruling of the discourse and that is not considered specification. The summary of the difference is that the common term establishes equality among the collection in the permissibility of each of them falling under the word when the inclusion of
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic classification from uṣūl al-fiqh. The author aims to expose the opponents' ignorance by clarifying the nature of words that cover multiple referents. Such words are divided into two categories. First, a general term (al-‘āmm) like 'men' (al-rijāl): all individuals share the same definition (e.g., the definition of manhood), and language requires that every individual falls under the word's ruling. Excluding any individual would be a specification (takhṣīṣ) and would limit the generality. Second, a homonymous term (mushtarak) like 'eye' (al-‘ayn): its individuals have different definitions (e.g., the organ, a water spring, the sun's disk). For such words, the discourse does not require covering all possible meanings; only one specific meaning is intended, determined by contextual evidence. Excluding other meanings is not considered specification because the term never had generality over all meanings. This distinction is agreed upon by scholars of uṣūl. The final sentence, though incomplete, summarizes that in a common term, all possible referents are equal in terms of possibility of being meant, but that possibility is resolved by evidence.

الحدود
أهل الأصول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All words that refer to a collection are general and require inclusion of all referents.
Correction: Homonymous words (mushtarak) like 'eye' have multiple distinct meanings and do not have generality; their intended meaning must be determined by evidence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the key difference between a general term like 'al-rijal' and a homonym like 'al-ayn'?
A) General terms include all individuals; homonyms include only one individual.
B) General terms require language to include all individuals with the same definition; homonyms require evidence to determine which meaning is intended.
C) Homonyms have no meaning; general terms always have many meanings.
D) General terms are always plural; homonyms are always singular.

Key Takeaway
General terms cover all individuals sharing the same definition, while homonyms require external evidence to specify which distinct meaning is intended.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quran or hadith, do you assume that every word that could have multiple meanings must include all of them? How might this lesson change how you approach verses about the 'hand' of Allah or the 'eye' of Allah? Are you willing to let the context—and the scholars—guide you to the intended meaning, even if it challenges your initial assumption of generality?
?
Think of a time when you misunderstood a command from Allah because you assumed a word was general when it was actually homonymous, or vice versa. What was the consequence? Will you now take the time to examine each term's classification before acting on it, even if it slows you down?
?
The author accuses his opponents of 'ignorance of the contexts of discourse.' Where in your own life might you be guilty of ignoring the context of a divine address, insisting on a meaning that fits your desires rather than what the evidence demands? What step will you take today to correct this?

Logic and Technical Terms

General vs. Common Terms
The author divides words into general ('amm) and common (mushtarak) categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic scholars classify words that cover multiple referents into general ('amm) and common (mushtarak) terms, and why this distinction matters for interpreting sacred texts.

Core Idea
Words with multiple referents are not all general — some are common, and only the latter need evidence to pick one meaning.

In Islamic legal theory (usul al-fiqh) and theology (kalam), words that apply to more than one thing require careful classification. Not every multi-referent word is 'general'; some are 'common' and behave differently. This passage from Imam al-Nasafi introduces this crucial division, using clear examples to illustrate the difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note how the author defines two categories at the very beginning.
The passage is dense; take time to understand the example of 'men' vs. 'eye'.
2
Identify the defining difference
The key distinction is whether the individuals covered by the word share identical definitions (general) or have different definitions (common). Identify this in the examples.
General terms like 'men' have a single definition for all individuals; common terms like 'eye' have multiple possible definitions.
3
Apply the practical rule
Understand the consequence: for a general term, excluding some individuals is 'particularization'; for a common term, the intended meaning must be determined by evidence, and excluding others is not particularization.
This affects how scholars interpret texts that use such words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : إن اللفظ المستولى على المسميات ينقسم إلى قسمين: أحدهما ما ينتظم جمعا كانت أفراده متفقة الحدود كقولنا : الرجال، فإن كل شخص يوافق غيره من الأشخاص الداخلة تحت هذا اللفظ في حد الرجولية وحد الرجل، وهذا هو العام المقتضي دخوله كل فرد من أفراده تحته تقتضيه اللغة، وخروج البعض منها عن حكم اللفظ يوجب خصوصا في الخطاب وسلبًا لعمومه. والآخر : ما يتناول جمعًا كانت أفراده مختلفة الحدود كلفظة العين، فإنها وإن كانت تقع على الآلة الباصرة وينبوع الماء وقرص الشمس وغير ذلك، فإنه لا شك أن حدودها مختلفة وأنواعها متغايرة، وما كان هذا سبيله لا يقتضي الخطاب الوارد بمثل هذه اللفظة الاشتغال على كل ما يصح دخوله تحت هذه اللفظة بل المراد هو الواحد منها غير عين، ويتوقف تعينه على وجود الدليل، ويسمى هذا النوع مشتركا، ولا عموم له بإجماع أهل الأصول، وعند تعين البعض مرادًا يخرج ما وراءه عن حكم الخطاب ولا يعد ذلك تخصيصا، وحاصل الفرق أن المشترك تثبت المساواة بين الجمع في جواز دخول كل من ذلك تحت اللفظ عند انتفاء دخول أغياره مما لا يجوز تناول اللفظ إياه لولا دخول غيره فيه، وفي العموم يجوز دخول كل فرد في اللفظ وإن دخل أغياره من أشخاص نوعه فيه.
We say: The word that dominates referents is divided into two types: one of them is what comprises a collection whose individuals have identical definitions, like our saying 'men', for every person agrees with others of the persons included under this word in the definition of manhood and the definition of man, and this is the general (al-ʿāmm) which requires the inclusion of every individual of its individuals under it as the language requires, and the exclusion of some of them from the rule of the word necessitates particularity (khuṣūṣ) in the discourse and the stripping of its generality. And the other: what comprises a collection whose individuals have different definitions, like the word 'eye' (al-ʿayn), for although it applies to the seeing organ, the spring of water, the disk of the sun, and other things, there is no doubt that their definitions are different and their types are diverse, and what is like this does not require the discourse occurring with such a word to cover everything that can validly fall under this word, but rather the intended meaning is one of them not specified, and its determination depends on the existence of evidence, and this type is called common (mushtarak), and it has no generality (ʿumūm) by consensus of the scholars of uṣūl, and when some [meaning] is determined as intended, what is beyond it exits from the rule of the discourse and that is not considered particularization (takhṣīṣ). And the summary of the difference is that the common term establishes equality among the collection in the permissibility of each of them falling under the word when the entry of what is other than them is absent, such that the word cannot apply to it were it not for the entry of something else into it, while in the general term it is permissible for every individual to fall under the word even if what is other than it from individuals of its type enters into it.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage classifies words that cover multiple referents into two types: general (ʿāmm) and common (mushtarak). The author first defines the general term: it includes individuals that share an identical definition, such as 'men' where every person shares the same definition of manhood. For a general term, any exclusion of an individual is considered particularization (takhṣīṣ) and strips the word of its generality. Next, the common term: it covers individuals with different definitions, like the word 'eye' (al-ʿayn) which can refer to the seeing organ, a water spring, or the sun's disk. For such terms, the discourse does not automatically include all possible referents; instead, one specific meaning is intended and must be determined by evidence. This type is called mushtarak and has no generality by consensus. When one meaning is determined, other referents are excluded from the rule without being considered particularization. The author then summarizes the difference: in a common term, all referents are equally possible for entry under the word only as long as no other referent enters; if another referent enters, the word may not apply to the first. In a general term, every individual can enter the word even if other individuals of the same type also enter. This distinction is crucial for later arguments about terms like 'thing' (shay') when applied to God and creation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A word covering multiple referents is always general.
Correction: When the referents have different definitions, the word is common, not general, and requires evidence to specify the intended meaning.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a general term (ʿāmm) from a common term (mushtarak) according to the author?
A) A general term has individuals with identical definitions, while a common term has individuals with different definitions.
B) A general term requires evidence to determine its meaning, while a common term does not.
C) A common term always includes all its referents, while a general term does not.
D) A general term is used only for human categories, while a common term applies to inanimate objects.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between general and common terms is fundamental: general terms have individuals sharing one definition, while common terms cover multiple definitions and require external evidence to determine the intended meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you listen to debates about God's attributes, do you automatically assume that any word used for both God and creation is general in meaning? This lesson shows that words like 'eye' are common, not general. Can you identify a theological term you may have misunderstood in this way? How will you now approach such terms differently?
?
The passage says a common term 'requires evidence' to determine which meaning is intended. In your own study of Islamic theology, are you actively seeking evidence for ambiguous terms, or do you rely on assumptions? What is one word or concept whose meaning you need to reexamine with this lesson in mind?
?
Consider a time when you mistakenly assumed a word had only one meaning and later discovered it was polysemous. Did that error affect your understanding of a religious text? This lesson challenges you to take responsibility for how you interpret words—will you commit to verifying meanings before drawing conclusions about Allah and His attributes?

The Word 'Thing' (Shay') is Not a Genus Term
The author argues that 'shay'' is not a genus term and claiming homogeneity is kufr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the word 'thing' is not a genus term and why claiming homogeneity between God and creation is kufr.

Core Idea
Calling God a 'thing' does not make Him one genus with creatures.

The word 'thing' (shay') is commonly used for both God and creatures, but this does not imply they belong to the same genus. In this passage, the author of the Tabṣira argues that the difference between necessary and contingent existence is so vast that 'thing' functions like a shared noun, not a genus term. Claiming homogeneity would be disbelief.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of word usage
The passage begins with a principle: a word may include a thing only if the context allows. Read that initial part and note the distinction between 'inclusion' and 'generality'.
This sets up the logic for the rest.
2
Analyze the word 'shay'' as a shared noun
The author compares God and the world as necessary vs. contingent existence. See how he argues that 'shay'' works like shared nouns because the difference is absolute.
3
Understand the consequence of claiming a genus
If 'shay'' were a genus, it would imply homogeneity between eternal and temporal, which is kufr. Reflect on why this is a serious theological error.
This is the punchline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مما لا يجوز تناول اللفظ إياه لولا دخول غيره فيه، وفي العموم يجوز دخول كل فرد في اللفظ وإن دخل أغياره من أشخاص نوعه فيه، وبعد الوقوف على هذه الجملة نقول: لفظة الشيء وإن كانت عبارة عن الموجود عندنا، والله تعالى بصفاته موجود والعالم حديث فيكون جائز الوجود، واختلاف ما بين واجب الوجود وجائز الوجود فوق اختلاف ما بين عين الشمس وينبوع الماء والآلة الباصرة بجواز الشبه والمساواة بين هذه الأشياء بكثير من المعاني، واستحالة الشبه بين القديم والمحدث بمعنى من المعاني، فكانت لفظة الشيء إذا أريد بها القديم لا يجوز دخول المحدث تحته، وإذا أريد بها المحدث يمتنع دخول القديم تحته كما في الأسماء المشتركة، وعن هذا قلنا: إن الشيء ليس باسم جنس، والله تعالى وإن أطلقت عليه لفظة الشيء ليس من جنس العالم ولو كانت لفظة الشيء عامة في القديم والحديث لكانت اسم جنس لما تحتها وكان القديم نوعاً منه والمحدث نوعًا آخر، فيختلفان نوعًا ويستويان جنسًا، والقول بإثبات المجانسة بين القديم والمحدث كفر فتبين بما ذكرنا وظهر أن الأمر على ما قررنا.
That which it is not permissible to apply the word to without the inclusion of something else in it, and in generality it is permissible for every individual to enter into the word even if others of its species enter into it. After understanding this statement, we say: The word 'thing' (shay'), although it is an expression for the existent (al-mawjūd) in our view, and Allah Most High with His attributes is existent, and the world is temporally originated (ḥādith), so it is contingent existence (jā'iz al-wujūd). And the difference between necessary existence (wājib al-wujūd) and contingent existence is greater than the difference between the sun itself and a water spring and the seeing instrument due to the possibility of resemblance and equality between these things in many meanings, and the impossibility of resemblance between the eternal (qadīm) and the temporal (muḥdath) in any meaning. Thus, the word 'thing' if intended for the eternal, it is not permissible for the temporal to enter under it, and if intended for the temporal, it is impossible for the eternal to enter under it, like in shared nouns (al-asmā' al-mushtaraka). And because of this we said: Indeed, 'thing' is not a genus noun (ism jins). And Allah Most High, even if the word 'thing' is applied to Him, He is not of the genus of the world. And if the word 'thing' were general (ʿām) regarding the eternal and the temporal, it would be a genus noun for what is under it, and the eternal would be a species (nawʿ) of it and the temporal another species. They would differ in species and be equal in genus. And the statement affirming homogeneity (mujānasa) between the eternal and the temporal is unbelief (kufr). Thus, it is clarified by what we have mentioned and it is apparent that the matter is as we established.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the term 'shay'' (thing). The author first establishes a principle about the use of words and generality: a word may include a thing only if that thing is not excluded by the context. Then he analyzes the word 'shay''. He states that it denotes existence (mawjūd). God is existent, the world is contingent existence. The difference between necessary and contingent existence is immense, far greater than any resemblance between physical objects. Therefore, 'shay'' when applied to God cannot include the contingent, and vice versa. It acts like a shared noun (ism mushtarak). Thus, it is not a genus term. If it were a genus, the eternal and temporal would be two species under the same genus, implying homogeneity (mujānasa) between them, which is kufr. The conclusion is that the matter is as established.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling God a 'thing' implies He belongs to the same genus as created things.
Correction: The word 'thing' is a shared noun (mushtarak) meaning it is used equivocally for different categories; it does not indicate a common genus.

Checkpoint
What is the ruling (ḥukm) on claiming that the word 'shay'' is a genus term that encompasses both the eternal and the temporal?

B) It is forbidden but not kufr
C) It is kufr
D) It is recommended

Key Takeaway
The word 'thing' is not a genus term; it is a shared noun because the necessary and contingent existence are incomparably different, and claiming homogeneity is kufr.

Refutation of Objection on Servant's Knowledge and Power
Al-Nasafi refutes the objection that knowledge of action is possible for the servant thus power is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Nasafi's refutation of the objection that the possibility of knowledge for the servant implies the possibility of creative power.

Core Idea
Possibility of a condition does not guarantee possibility of the conditioned.

In this passage, Al-Nasafi responds to an objection from opponents who argue that if knowledge (a condition for power) is possible for the servant, then power itself must also be possible. He refutes this by shifting the focus to the actual disputed point: actions performed in ignorance, which the opponents already claimed are created by servants but were proven impossible. He further argues that creative power is beyond human capacity, and that the existence of a condition does not necessitate the existence of the conditioned if the latter is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The opponents present a conditional argument: if knowledge of an action from certain aspects is possible for the servant, then power (which depends on knowledge) is also possible.
Notice how the opponents use the concept of 'possible' (ja'iz) to build their case.
2
Examine the first part of the refutation
Al-Nasafi points out that the objection deviates from the actual point of dispute. The dispute is about actions that occur without the servant's knowledge, not about actions where knowledge is present.
The author emphasizes that the opponents already claimed such actions are created by servants, but impossibility was proven.
3
Analyze the second and third arguments
Even if knowledge were possible in general, humans cannot comprehend the act of bringing from non-existence to existence, so the condition for creative power is inconceivable. Moreover, the existence of a condition does not entail the existence of the conditioned if the conditioned is impossible.
This is a powerful logical move: possibility of the condition does not guarantee possibility of the conditioned.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how Al-Nasafi uses both contextual (the actual dispute) and logical arguments to refute the objection. This demonstrates the method of kalam: precise definition of the disputed point and careful reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض، والرد عليه. فإن قالوا: من الجائزات ثبوت العلم للعبد بفعله من هذه الوجوه، وإذا كان ثبوت الشرط من الجائزات كان ثبوت القدرة من الجائزات. قيل لهم هذا عدول عن الكلام في المتنازع ، فإن المتنازع بيننا وبينكم وقع في هذه الأفعال الحاصلة مع الجهل من العباد بها بهذه الوجوه، فادعيتم أنتم كونها مخلوقة للعباد، ونحن أثبنا ذلك وثبتنا بالدليل استحالته بحمد الله تعالى. فبعد ذلك تصور ثبوت هذه الشريطة في الجملة مما لا ينفعكم، على أنا نقول: ليس في مقدور البشر الاطلاع على الإخراج من العدم إلى الوجود فلا يتصور شريطة قدرة التخليق، على أن ثبوت شريطة الشيء مما لا يوجب ثبوته إذا امتنع
Objection and its refutation. For if they say: 'It is among the possible things that knowledge for the servant of his action from these aspects exists, and if the existence of the condition is among the possible things, then the existence of power is among the possible things.' It is said to them: This is a deviation from the discourse concerning the disputed point. For the disputed point between us and you concerns these actions that occur with the servants' ignorance of them in these aspects. You claim that they are created by the servants, and we have proven the impossibility of that by the proof, praise be to Allah. So after that, the conceptualization of the existence of this condition in general does not benefit you. Moreover, we say: It is not within the capability of humans to have insight into bringing from non-existence to existence, so the condition of the power of creation cannot be conceptualized. Moreover, the existence of the condition of something does not necessitate its existence if it is impossible.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This is an objection and its refutation. The opponent argues that since it is possible (ja'iz) for the servant to have knowledge of his action from these aspects, and since knowledge is a condition for power, it is also possible for the servant to have power. The author refutes: This shifts the topic; the dispute is about actions that occur with ignorance. The opponent has already claimed these actions are created by servants, but impossibility has been proven. Even if knowledge were possible in general, it does not apply here. Furthermore, humans cannot comprehend bringing from non-existence to existence, so the condition for creative power cannot be conceived. Lastly, the existence of a condition does not necessitate the existence of the conditioned if the conditioned is impossible.

Key Terms
جائز
شرط
قدرة تخليق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if knowledge is possible, then power must follow.
Correction: The author clarifies that the actual context is ignorance, and creative power is beyond human capacity. Moreover, the possibility of a condition does not guarantee the possibility of the conditioned if the conditioned is impossible.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that knowledge and power are possible for the servant?
A) By saying it is impossible for humans to have knowledge.
B) By saying the dispute is about actions that occur with ignorance, and the condition of creative power is impossible for humans.
C) By saying that knowledge is not a condition for power.
D) By saying that the servant's power is independent.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nasafi refutes the objection by showing that the possibility of a condition (knowledge) does not imply the possibility of the conditioned (power) when the conditioned is impossible and the dispute is about ignorant actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The opponents shifted the topic to avoid the real issue. When in your own life do you avoid the real issue—especially regarding your relationship with Allah—by focusing on something else? What is the real issue you are avoiding right now, and what is one step you will take today to face it?
?
Al-Nasafi says humans cannot conceive of creation. How does your inability to create even a single atom humble your pride? If you truly believed that you can create nothing, what would change about your attitude towards your own achievements and your reliance on Allah?

Conditional Impossibility in Kalam
The author concludes a proof against creative power by explaining conditional impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical principle that when a thing's existence depends on a condition, its impossibility in the condition's absence does not imply the condition's impossibility, only its absence.

Core Idea
Absence, not impossibility, of the condition suffices for the conditioned's impossibility.

This passage from Imam al-Nasafi's Tabsirat al-Adilla sharpens a key logical tool in kalam: what does it mean for something to be 'impossible' due to a missing condition? The author is refuting an opponent's claim about creative power by clarifying that a contingent thing's non-existence when its condition is absent does not force us to declare the condition itself impossible. Understanding this distinction is crucial for assessing proofs about divine attributes and human capacity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full literal translation. Let the author's argument structure become clear before analyzing details.
Notice the repeated terms 'sharīṭa' (condition) and 'muʿallaq' (contingent thing).
2
Identify the main claim
Find where the author states the impossibility of a contingent thing when its condition is absent. Underline that sentence.
The claim is about the relationship between condition and conditioned.
3
Distinguish two concepts
The author distinguishes between 'impossibility of the condition' and 'absence of the condition'. Which does he say is required for the contingent thing to be impossible?
This is the core logical point.
4
Review the commentary
Read the teacher commentary which explains the passage in simpler terms. Compare it with your own understanding.
Make sure you grasp why the opponents are accused of deviating from the real question.
5
Apply the principle
Think of an everyday example: if a plant's growth depends on water, does the plant's death in drought make water impossible? Or merely absent?
This helps internalize the logical distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثبوته بدليل آخر، وقد امتنع هاهنا بدليل آخر، نبين ذلك إذا انتهينا إلى إقامة الدليل على أن إثبات قدرة التخليق تؤدي إلى المحال، ونحن نتكلم معكم في حال انعدام العلم الذي هو شريطة ثبوت القدرة فندعي استحالة ثبوتها في حال انعدام العلم الذي هو شريطة ثبوتها وامتناع ما تعلق ثبوته بوجود شرط يكون ثابتا عند انعدام الشرط، ولا يفتقر امتناع المعلق إلى امتناع الشرط بل يفتقر إلى انعدامه، لأن شرط وجود المعلق وجود نفس الشرط لا إمكان وجوده، وإذا عرف هذا تبين حيد الخصوم عن محز السؤال باشتغالهم بمثل هذا الخيال، والله الموفق.
Its proof is by another proof, and it has been impossible here by another proof; we will explain this when we reach the establishment of the proof that proving creative power leads to absurdity. And we are discussing with you in the state of absence of knowledge which is the condition for the existence of power, so we claim the impossibility of its existence in the state of absence of knowledge which is the condition for its existence, and the impossibility of what is contingent upon the existence of a condition being existent when the condition is absent. The impossibility of the contingent thing does not require the impossibility of the condition, but rather requires its absence, because the condition for the existence of the contingent thing is the existence of the condition itself, not the possibility of its existence. When this is known, the deviation of the opponents from the core of the question becomes clear by their occupation with such fantasy. God grants success.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage concludes a proof regarding the impossibility of establishing creative power for the servant. The author argues that if a thing's existence depends on a condition (knowledge), then when that condition is absent, the thing cannot exist. The impossibility of the conditioned thing when its condition is absent does not require the impossibility of the condition itself; rather, it only requires the absence of the condition. The condition for the existence of the conditioned thing is the actual existence of the condition, not merely its possibility. The author then states that once this principle is understood, it becomes clear that the opponents have deviated from the real issue by occupying themselves with irrelevant fantasies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if a contingent thing is impossible when its condition is absent, then the condition itself must be impossible.
Correction: The author clarifies that the impossibility of the contingent thing only requires the absence of the condition, not its impossibility. The condition may be possible but simply absent.

B) The possibility of the condition
C) The absence of the condition
D) The existence of the condition

Key Takeaway
The absence of a condition suffices to make the conditioned thing impossible; the condition itself may still be possible.

What condition for righteousness are you neglecting right now, and how will you restore it?
