When the Question of Separate Agents Applies
Nasafi responds to a question about non-believer and walking, distinguishing attributes from essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between attributes separate from the essence and attributes inherent in the essence, and why the objection about multiple agents only applies to the former.

Core Idea
Attributes separate from essences can be assigned to different agents; inherent attributes cannot.

This passage addresses a subtle objection about the actions of non-believers and walking. Imam Nasafi clarifies that the question of separate agents for attribute and essence only makes sense when the attribute is distinct from the described thing, like in bodies. For accidents, where the attribute is inseparable, the objection collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between the two cases: bodies and accidents.
2
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two distinct scenarios: one where the attribute is 'beyond the meaning of the described' (as in bodies) and one where attributes 'refer back to their essences' (as in accidents). Find the Arabic phrases that introduce each case.
The first case is introduced by 'فيما كانت الصفة وراء معنى الموصوف', the second by 'فأما في الأعراض'.
3
Understand the logic
Ask yourself why the objection can be raised in the first case but not the second. The commentary explains that when attribute and essence are distinct, one could imagine separate agents, but evidence shows that is false. For accidents, the objection is simply invalid because the attribute is the essence itself.
This connects to the broader issue of tawhid and the impossibility of multiple creators.
4
Reflect on the key terms
Note the key terms: الصفة (attribute), الموصوف (described thing), الأعراض (accidents), الموجدين (agents of existence). Understanding these terms helps grasp the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
غير الكافر والماشي؟ قيل : هذا السؤال إنما يتحقق فيما كانت الصفة وراء معنى الموصوف، كما في الأجسام وصفاتها فيتخبط إنسان فيقول: تولى فاعل إيجاد الصفة وآخر إيجاد الموصوف فلا يكون محيلاً في نفس الدعوى، وإن كان الدليل يوجب لدى التحقيق فساد قول من يدعي تعدد الموجدين، فأما في الأعراض التي كانت صفاتها في الحقيقة راجعة إلى ذواتها فلا يستقيم مثل هذا الكلام.
As for the non-believer and the walking one? It was said: This question only occurs when the attribute is beyond the meaning of the described, as in bodies and their attributes, so a person may become confused and say: One agent took the creation of the attribute and another the creation of the described, so it is not impossible in the claim itself, even if the evidence necessitates upon investigation the corruption of the claim of those who assert multiplicity of agents. As for accidents whose attributes in reality refer back to their essences, such speech is not valid.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage responds to a question about how a non-believer and a walking person are related to their actions. It explains that the question applies only when the attribute is separate from the essence, as in bodies and their attributes. In such a case, one might imagine separate agents for the attribute and the essence. But for accidents (sing. 'arad), their attributes are inseparable from their essences, so the objection does not hold.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection about multiple agents for an attribute and its described applies universally to all actions and attributes, including accidents.
Correction: The objection only applies when the attribute is conceptually separate from the essence, as in bodies. For accidents, the attribute is not separate, so the objection does not arise.

Checkpoint

A) When the attribute is inherent in the described
B) When the attribute is beyond the meaning of the described
C) When the described is a substance
D) When the attribute is an accident

Key Takeaway
The objection about multiple agents for an attribute and its described only applies when the attribute is separate from the essence, not for accidents where attribute and essence are one.

What changes in your awareness of Allah's power?

The Essence as the Cause of Similarity and Difference
Nasafi states the cause of similarity and difference is the essence and refutes al-Ka'bi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that in Maturidi theology, similarity and difference between things are determined by their essences, not by attributes.

Core Idea
Similarity and difference are rooted in the essence, not in attributes.

This lesson presents a key principle from Imam Nasafi: the cause of similarity and difference between two things is their essence, not any attribute. Nasafi argues that essences remain unchanged regardless of attributes, and he criticizes al-Ka'bi for misunderstanding this principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage below and its literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms, especially 'illa (cause), dhāt (essence), and the contrast with attributes.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the sentence that states the cause of similarity and difference. Notice that it is the essence itself, not attributes.
The phrase 'علة الاشتباه والاختلاف لما كان هو الذات' is the core thesis.
3
Understand the argument
Nasafi explains that essences do not change when attributes differ or unite. This shows that similarity and difference are inherent in the essences themselves.
The argument uses 'لا تتغير' and 'لا يتحدان' to emphasize stability.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Nasafi accuses al-Ka'bi of either ignorance or deception for objecting to this truth. Consider why this principle matters for kalam discussions about identity and distinction.
Al-Ka'bi was a Mu'tazilite scholar; this refutation highlights a key difference between Maturidi and Mu'tazili views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
علة الاشتباه والاختلاف لما كان هو الذات، وباختلاف ما وراء الذاتين في المعاني لا تتغير الذاتان ولا باتحاد ما وراء الذاتين من المعنى يتحدان، ولا يتغير ما هو من مقتضيات الذاتين من التشابه والاختلاف. وعرف بهذا حيد الكعبي في الاعتراض عن الحقائق إما جهلاً منه بذلك، وإما تمويها على الضعفة.
The cause of similarity and difference, since it is the essence, and by the difference of what is beyond the two essences in attributes the two essences do not change, nor by the union of what is beyond the two essences in attribute do they unite, and what is from the requirements of the two essences of similarity and difference does not change. And by this al-Ka'bi was known to err in objecting to realities, either out of ignorance or out of dissimulation to the weak.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage defines the principle that the cause (‛illa) of similarity (tashābuh) and difference (ikhtilāf) is the essence (dhāt) itself. Nasafi argues that essences do not change due to varying attributes, nor do they unite due to shared attributes; rather, similarity and difference are inherent in the essences themselves. He then criticizes al-Ka'bi for missing this point, suggesting his objection to realities stems from either ignorance or deceit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Similarity and difference between two things are determined by their attributes.
Correction: Nasafi insists that the cause is the essence itself; attributes do not affect whether essences are similar or different.

Checkpoint

A) Their attributes
B) Their essences
C) Their actions
D) Their names

Key Takeaway
Similarity and difference are inherent in essences, not derived from attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Nasafi's Dialectic on Similarity of Movements
Dialectical argument on similarity of movements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi uses dialectical reasoning to show that similarity and difference of actions are grounded in essences, not accidental attributes like command or place.

Core Idea
Similarity and difference depend on essence, not on accidental commands or places.

This passage presents a dialectical exchange (jadal) typical of kalam. Nasafi considers whether two movements commanded in different places are similar or different. By examining both possibilities, he refutes the idea that accidental attributes determine essential similarity. Follow the reasoning carefully to see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the dialectical scenario
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of the argument. Notice the back-and-forth structure: a question is posed, then two possible answers are examined.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The argument considers two responses: (1) the movements are similar because both are commanded, or (2) they are different because they occur in different places. Trace how Nasafi responds to each.
3
Analyze the concept of necessary concomitants
Nasafi argues that command and prohibition are not necessary concomitants of movement, while place is. Understand the distinction between necessary and accidental attributes.
A necessary concomitant is something without which the thing cannot exist.
4
Grasp the broader implication about essence vs. accident
The argument extends to show that obedience/disobedience are accidental and do not affect essential similarity. Nasafi uses the example of rest and movement being both acts of obedience to reinforce his point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال له: ما قولك فيما إذا كانت حركة مأموراً بها في مكان ثم أمر بحركة أخرى في مكان آخر، أهاتان متجانسان (أو) مختلفان؟ فإن قال: هما متجانسان متشابهان. قيل: فما بالهما لم يختلفا باختلاف الأمكنة، واختلفا باختلاف الأمر والنهي؟ أن الأمر والنهي ليسا من القرائن اللازمة للحركة لتصور وجودها بدونهما وحصولهما غير مأمور بهما ولا منهي عنهما، والمكان من القرائن اللازمة لاستحالة وجود الحركة والسكون بدون المكان. وإن قال: هما مختلفان لاختلاف الأمكنة قلنا فما بالهما اختلافتا أنهما طاعتان ولم يوجب اجتماعهما في الاتصاف بكونهما طاعتين تشابههما وإن اختلفت الأمكنة وأوجب افتراقهما في الاتصاف بكونهما طاعة أو معصية اختلافهما مع استوائهما في الذات الذي هو علة للاشتباه. وكذا إذا أمر بسكون ثم أمر بحركة هل أوجب اجتماعهما في الاتصاف بكونهما طاعتين اشتباههما أم لا؟ فإن قال : نعم، أوجب الاشتباه بين المتضادين مع اقتضاء ذاتيهما الاختلاف بينهما والذات المقتضى لذلك موجود وإن قال : لا ، أبطل الاتفاق من حيث الطاعة أن تكون موجبا للتشابه عند الاختلاف في الذات، وكذا اختلافهما من حيث الطاعة لا يوجب الاختلاف بينهما عند استواء الذاتين و اقتضائهما تشابهها، والله والموفق.
Then it is said to him: What do you say about when a movement is commanded in a place, then another movement is commanded in another place; are these two similar or different? If he says: They are similar. It is said: Why then do they not differ due to difference of places, but differ due to difference of command and prohibition? Because command and prohibition are not necessary concomitants of movement, since movement can be conceived without them and it can occur not commanded or prohibited, while place is a necessary concomitant because movement and rest cannot exist without place. And if he says: They are different due to difference of places, we say: Why then are they both acts of obedience and does their sharing the attribute of being acts of obedience not necessitate their similarity despite different places, while their separation in being attributed as obedience or disobedience necessitates their difference despite equality in essence which is the cause of similarity? Likewise, if one commands rest then commands movement, does their sharing the attribute of being acts of obedience necessitate their similarity or not? If he says: Yes, it necessitates similarity between two opposites despite their essences requiring difference, and the essence that requires that exists. If he says: No, he invalidates the agreement from the aspect of obedience being a cause of similarity when there is difference in essence, and likewise their difference from the aspect of obedience does not necessitate difference between them when essences are equal and require similarity. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument (jadal) refuting an opponent's position. Nasafi poses a hypothetical case: if two movements are commanded in different places, are they similar or different? He examines both possible answers. If the opponent says they are similar because both are commanded, Nasafi points out that command is not a necessary concomitant (qarīna lāzima) of movement, while place is; so they should not be similar if places differ. If the opponent says they are different because places differ, Nasafi responds that then two acts of obedience should be similar regardless of place, since obedience is a shared attribute. He extends the argument to opposite acts (rest and movement) being both acts of obedience, showing that if the opponent claims similarity due to obedience, that would force similarity between opposites, which contradicts their essences. If the opponent denies similarity, he undermines his own principle. The argument reinforces that similarity and difference are grounded in essences, not accidental attributes like command or place.

Key Terms
حركة
مكان
أمر
نهي
طاعة
معصية
متضادان
قرائن لازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being commanded or prohibited makes actions similar or different in essence.
Correction: Nasafi shows that command and prohibition are accidental (not necessary concomitants) and cannot determine essential similarity; essence itself is the criterion.

Checkpoint
In the dialectical argument, what does Nasafi say about command and prohibition in relation to movement?
A) They are necessary concomitants
B) They are not necessary concomitants
C) They are the cause of similarity
D) They are the cause of difference

Key Takeaway
Nasafi demonstrates that accidental attributes like command or place cannot determine essential similarity; only the essence (dhāt) does.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you judged two actions as the same because they both seemed 'obedient' or 'good', but they were essentially different. How did that superficial judgment affect your spiritual growth? Will you commit to examining the essence of actions before labeling them?
?
When you evaluate your own deeds, do you give disproportionate weight to external circumstances (like where or when you performed them) rather than the intrinsic quality of the act? How can you shift your focus to what truly matters in your intentions and actions?
?
This lesson exposes the inconsistency of holding two contradictory standards. In your daily life, where are you inconsistent? For example, expecting leniency for yourself but strictness for others. What concrete step will you take today to align your judgments with the truth of essences?

Essential Similarity of Contingent Things
Imam Nasafi argues that contingent things agree in the meaning of contingency despite accidental differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why contingent things are essentially similar in their contingency despite accidental differences, and how this guards against anthropomorphic comparisons between Creator and creatures.

Core Idea
Accidental differences do not negate the essential similarity of contingent things in being contingent.

Imam Nasafi addresses a potential objection to the argument for the contingency of the world. If contingent things can differ in how they occur (some with effort, some without), does that undermine their essential similarity? This passage shows that contingency—existence after non-existence—is a single meaning that unites all originated things. Accidental attributes are external to the essence and do not affect that core similarity. Read carefully as Nasafi uses an analogy with blackness to drive home the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text aloud or silently. Notice the key terms: محدث, حدوث, اشتباه, ذات, سواد.
The passage is a continuous argument against those who claim accidental differences break similarity.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
First, Nasafi states that Creator and contingent are dissimilar because the Creator is self-knowing. Second, two contingents are similar in contingency. The rest is an objection and response.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objector says one contingent occurs with treatment/effort, another without. Nasafi replies that this does not affect contingency, which is simply existence after non-existence. Treatment/effort are external.
Notice the analogy: blackness belonging to Zayd vs. not belonging does not change the blackness itself.
4
Reflect on the lesson's conclusion
Contingent things are essentially similar in contingency regardless of accidental differences. This supports the proof of a single Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى وراء المشتبهين والباري عالم بنفسه وليس المحدث عالما بنفسه، فلا يكون بينهما مشابهة على أصلهم. فأما المحدثان فلم يكونا ثم كانا فاتفقا بأنفسهما في معنى الحدوث، وقول القائل: أحدهما حدث لا بعلاج ولا تعب، والآخر حدث بعلاج وتعب، ليس بنفي الاشتباه عن المحدثين بأنفسهما إذ ليس الحدوث إلا الوجود من العدم، وهذا المعنى لا يختلف في حق حادث وحادث وإن كان أحدهما حدث بعلاج وتعب والآخر حدث لا بعلاج وتعب، إذ العلاج والتعب معنى وراء نفس الحادث ونفي معنى وراء نفس الحادث وإثبات ذلك المعنى في حق حادث لا يوجب تغيرا في ذاتيهما. ألا ترى أن من قال: هذا سواد لزيد، وهذا سواد ليس لزيد لا الاختلاف بين السوادين ولا زوال الاشتباه، فكذا هذا.
A meaning beyond the two similar things: the Creator is knowing by Himself, and the contingent is not knowing by Himself, so there is no similarity between them according to their principle. As for two contingent things, they were not then they were, so they agree in themselves in the meaning of contingency. The statement of one who says: 'One of them occurred without treatment and effort, and the other occurred with treatment and effort' does not negate the similarity between the two contingent things in themselves, since contingency is nothing but existence after non-existence. This meaning does not differ in the case of one contingent and another, even if one occurred with treatment and effort and the other without treatment and effort, because treatment and effort are a meaning beyond the contingent itself, and negating a meaning beyond the contingent itself and affirming that meaning in the case of a contingent does not necessitate a change in their essences. Do you not see that if someone says: 'This blackness belongs to Zayd, and this blackness does not belong to Zayd,' there is no difference between the two blacknesses nor a removal of similarity? Likewise this.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage is a proof that contingent things (al-muhdathat) are essentially similar in their contingency (huduth), and accidental differences do not negate their essential similarity. The author begins by stating that the Creator (al-Bari) is knowing by Himself, while the contingent is not knowing by Himself, so according to their own principle (i.e., the anthropomorphists) there is no similarity between Creator and creature. Then he turns to two contingent things: they both come into existence after non-existence, so they agree in the meaning of contingency. If someone objects that one occurs with 'treatment and effort' and the other without, the author explains that this difference does not negate their essential similarity because 'treatment and effort' are external to the essence. The essence of both is contingency, which is the same in all contingent things. He reinforces this with an analogy: two instances of blackness (sawad) may differ in ownership (one belonging to Zayd, the other not), but the blackness itself does not differ, nor is similarity removed. So too, accidental attributes like the manner of occurrence do not affect the essential similarity of contingent things in being contingent.

Key Terms
محدث
حدوث
اشتباه
ذات
سواد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidental differences in how contingent things occur (e.g., with or without effort) mean they are not essentially similar.
Correction: Imam Nasafi shows that contingency is a single meaning (existence after non-existence), and accidental attributes like treatment/effort are external to the essence; they do not change the fact that both are contingent.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that two contingent things are essentially similar despite differences in how they occur?
A) Because both are created by God.
B) Because contingency (huduth) is a single meaning common to all originated things, and accidental attributes do not change the essence.
C) Because they both have the same shape.
D) Because they are both black.

Key Takeaway
Contingent things are essentially similar in their contingency because the meaning of contingency (existence after non-existence) is the same for all originated beings, and accidental attributes do not alter that core similarity.

Will you let this truth humble your pride today?
?

?
If you truly believed that all contingent things are essentially similar in their contingency, how would that change your view of your own accomplishments and failures? What would you say to yourself when you feel superior or inferior to others?

The General Name Objection Refuted
Refutation of the objection that motion's general name and specific attribute negate similarity between acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fact that motion is a general name that can be obedience or disobedience does not negate essential similarity between acts of motion.

Core Idea
Accidental attributes do not change the essence — similarity remains.

In this passage, Imam al-Nasafi refutes an objection that would undermine the Maturidi position on the similarity of accidental attributes. The objector claims that if 'motion' is a general name, and similarity only occurs through specific attributes (like obedience or disobedience), then two motions could not be essentially similar. The author responds by distinguishing between essential attributes and those established by divine command, providing two clear proofs to show that generality does not prevent similarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the objector's claim that motion is a general name and similarity is by specific attributes, so obedience/disobedience as specific attributes should negate similarity.
This sets up the problem.
2
Analyze the author's main argument
Identify the author's distinction between essential attributes (referring to the essence) and accidental attributes established by command. Notice that he says the essence is unchanged.
Ask yourself: why does this distinction resolve the objection?
3
Examine the two proofs
Study the stillness proof (sukun can also be obedience/disobedience) and the blackness analogy (two blacknesses are similar despite general name).
These proofs show that attributes like obedience are not essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: إن قولنا: حركة اسم عام والاشتباه يكون بالصفة الخاصة، وكونها طاعة ومعصية صفة خاصة في الحركة، لأنها تتنوع إلى الطاعة والمعصية. قلنا: قد مر إبطال هذا في مسألة التشبيه، ثم نقول: إن كون الحركة طاعة أو معصية ليس براجع إلى الذات بل ذلك اسم ثبت لورود الأمر به، فلا تغير به الصفة الراجعة إلى الذات لأن الذات لا تتغير به. ألا ترى أن ضد الحركة وهو السكون قد يوصف بهما. والذي يحقق هذا: اشتباه سواد زید بسواد عمرو، ولا يقال السواد اسم عام ينقسم باعتبار انقسام المحل فلا يكونان مشتبهين بعمومهما بل باشتباههما عندكم لما أن الانقسام لأجل المحل ليس براجع إلى الذات، فلم يوجب عمومه، فكذا هذا.
If they say: 'Our statement that motion is a general name, and similarity occurs by the specific attribute, and its being obedience or disobedience is a specific attribute of motion, because it is divided into obedience and disobedience.' We say: 'The refutation of this has already passed in the issue of tashbih (anthropomorphism). Then we say: that a motion being obedience or disobedience does not refer back to the essence; rather, that name is established due to the command regarding it, so the attribute referring back to the essence is not changed by it, because the essence is not changed by it. Do you not see that the opposite of motion, which is stillness, may be described by both? And what confirms this: the similarity of Zayd's blackness to 'Amr's blackness; it is not said that blackness is a general name divided by the division of the locus, so they are not similar by its generality but by their similarity, according to you, because division due to the locus does not refer back to the essence, so its generality does not necessitate (similarity). Likewise this.'
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector claims that if motion is a general name, then similarity between two motions occurs only by a specific attribute (like obedience or disobedience), not by the general concept. The author responds by first referring back to an earlier refutation in the issue of tashbih. Then he gives a new argument: the attributes of being obedience or disobedience are not essential (dhati) but are derived from divine command; they do not change the essence (al-dhat). He provides two proofs: first, stillness (sukun), the opposite of motion, can also be described as obedience or disobedience, showing these are external to the essence. Second, using the analogy of blackness (sawad): two instances of blackness are similar even though blackness is a general name; the division of blackness by its locus (e.g., belonging to Zayd or 'Amr) does not refer back to the essence, so generality does not prevent similarity. Therefore, the objection fails.

اسم عام

طاعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a general name (ism 'amm) prevents essential similarity between two instances of that name.
Correction: The author shows that similarity is not negated by generality because specific attributes that do not affect the essence (like obedience/disobedience) do not change the underlying essence.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the fact that motion can be obedience or disobedience not prevent essential similarity between two motions?
A) Because obedience and disobedience are not real attributes.
B) Because these attributes are derived from divine command and do not change the essence of motion.
C) Because motion is not a real accident.
D) Because all motions are the same.

Key Takeaway
General names do not prevent essential similarity; only attributes that refer to the essence matter.

Answering the Objection of Unintelligibility
The author responds to the claim that it is unintelligible for God to create the act while the human is the doer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Māturīdī response to the objection of unintelligibility relies on distinguishing rational proof from sensory imagination.

Core Idea
Rational proof, not imagination, determines what is intelligible in kalām.

This passage presents a classic kalām objection and response. The objectors claim that the idea of God creating an act while the human is the doer is unintelligible. The author counters by redefining 'intelligible' based on rational proof rather than sensory experience, and argues that what is unimaginable need not be impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection as presented: 'This is unintelligible – that Allah brings forth the act from non-existence while the servant is the doer.' Note the core claim.
The objection equates intelligibility with conceivability in the mind.
2
Identify the response's definitional move
The author challenges the premise by asking 'Why do you say it is unintelligible?' and then defines 'intelligible' as what is established by rational proof, not by sensory imagination.
This shifts the criterion from imagination to proof.
3
Understand the two proofs referenced
The author refers to earlier proofs: (1) the impossibility of anyone other than God having creative power, and (2) the servant having an act. These proofs establish the point rationally.
The response does not repeat the proofs but assumes them.
4
Grasp the limitation of imagination
The author explains that the objectors cannot conceive it because they have not experienced anything similar in the visible world. Imagination depends on sense perception, which is limited.
This is a key epistemological point: absence from imagination does not imply logical impossibility.
5
Note the conclusion
The author concludes that something being beyond imagination does not necessitate its impossibility when rational proof exists. This ends the refutation.
The passage is incomplete, but the conclusion is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتراض، والرد عليه]: فإن قالوا هذا غير معقول أن يكون الله تعالى مخرجًا للفعل من العدم إلى الوجود والعبد يكون فاعلاً. قلنا: لم قلتم أنه غير معقول، والمعقول ما قام عليه الدليل العقلي وقد قام فيما نحن فيه، فإن الدليل قد قام على استحالة ثبوت قدرة التخليق لغير الله سبحانه وتعالى، وقد قام على أن العبد له فعل، إلا أنه لا يتصور في أوهامكم ذلك، لأنكم لم تروا في الشاهد من له قدرة الفعل في محل قدرة غيره ومن له قدرة الاختراع ليفعل فعله الاختياري ويوجده في محل قدرته، فلم يتصور ذلك في أوهامكم عند قصور حواسكم عند الوقوف عليه، والوهم من نتائج الحس على ما سبق ذكره، وخروج الشيء عن الوهم مما لا يوجب استحالة ثبوته عند قيام الدليل على ثبوته.
Objection and its refutation: If they say: This is unintelligible – that Allah Most High brings forth the act from non-existence into existence while the servant is the doer. We say: Why do you say it is unintelligible? The intelligible is that which is established by rational proof, and rational proof has been established in this matter. For the proof has been established for the impossibility of attributing the power of creation to other than Allah, and it has been established that the servant has an act; however, this is not conceivable in your imaginations because you have not seen in the witnessed world anyone who has the power of action in the locus of another's power, nor anyone who has the power of origination to do his voluntary act and bring it into existence in the locus of his own power. So it could not be conceived in your imaginations due to the deficiency of your senses when confronted with it, and imagination is a result of sense perception as previously mentioned. And a thing's being beyond imagination does not necessitate the impossibility of its existence when proof for its existence is established.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage presents a third objection and its answer. The objection claims that the concept of God creating an act while the human is the doer is unintelligible (غير معقول). The author responds by defining 'intelligible' (معقول) as that which is established by rational proof, not by sense perception or imagination. He states that rational proof has already been given for two points: (1) the impossibility of anyone other than God having creative power (قدرة التخليق), and (2) the servant having an act. The reason the objectors find it inconceivable is that they have never experienced in the visible world (الشاهد) a case where one agent acts within the domain of another's power, or where the power of origination belongs to someone else. Their imagination (وهم) is limited by sense perception, as previously explained. He concludes that something being unimaginable does not make it impossible, as long as rational proof supports it. The passage is incomplete at the end, but the main point is clear.

Key Terms
معقول
وهم
دليل عقلي
شاهد
قدرة الاختراع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something cannot be imagined, it must be impossible.
Correction: The author explains that unintelligibility based on lack of sensory experience does not make something impossible; rational proof is the arbiter.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why do the objectors find the concept of God creating the human's act unintelligible?
A) Because rational proof contradicts it.
B) Because they have not experienced it in the visible world.
C) Because it is logically impossible.
D) Because the Quran denies it.

Key Takeaway
Unimaginability does not disprove something; rational proof is the arbiter of intelligibility in kalām.

Refutation of Multiple Motions in One Place: Nasafi's Argument
Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila argument for multiple motions in one place and declares it absurd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila claim that multiple motions can occur in the same place by showing that it leads to absurd consequences and contradicts rational principles.

Core Idea
Allowing two motions in one place leads to absurdity, refuting the Mu'tazila.

In this passage, Nasafi embarks on a systematic refutation of the Mu'tazila doctrine that two or more motions can coexist in the same spatial location. He begins with a logical principle about causality and then applies it to a concrete example of motion. The argument unfolds to show that the Mu'tazila position leads to contradictions that even reason rejects. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the causal premise
Nasafi starts with a conditional: if the cause were non-existent, the effect would be non-existent. This sets up the framework for analyzing motion as an effect of a cause.
This is a foundational principle in kalam.
2
Examine the motion example
He considers a part that traverses only one place. If we suppose two motions from two movers, it would be equally valid to suppose one motion from the unity of place. This shows the arbitrariness.
3
Extend to absurdity
Allowing two motions in one place logically permits a thousand motions in that same place, which is absurd. This is the key reductio ad absurdum.
The principle of 'what leads to absurdity is false' is applied.
4
Contemplate the consequences
Nasafi concludes that the Mu'tazila principles lead to contradictory allowances, such as one accident in many substrates or many accidents in one substrate with no effect. This is impossible, and he criticizes their departure from reason and religion.
The prayer at the end underscores the seriousness of the deviation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينعدم المعلول لو انعدم هو لا يكون علة بالثبوت، ولو كان لما كان به فكانت إحداهما غير موجبة للخروج فلم تكن حركة.
يحققه : أن هذا الجزء ما قطع إلا مكانا وحدا، ولو جاز لك أن تقول: وجدت حركتان لوجود محركين كان لغيرك أن يقول : لا بل وجدت حركة لاتحاد المكان الذي قطع ويقال له: لو جاز وجود حركتين لا يقطع بهما إلا مكان واحد لجاز وجود ألف حركة لا يقطع بهما إلا مكان واحد، وهو يلتزم هنا.
ويقول: لو حرك الجسم ألف نفر وأكثر حدث في كل جزء من أجزائه من الحركات بعدد الفاعلين، والقول بوجود ألف حركة لا يقطع بها إلا مكان واحد تجاهل، ولو جاز أن يخطر أحد هذا بباله لجاز لغيره أن يقول على القلب، ويجوز أن يقطع ألف مكان بحركة واحدة. وهذا كله هذيان، فاقتضت بهم أصولهم الفاسدة إلى أن جوزوا تارة قيام عرض واحد بألف محل وأكثر، وجوزوا تارة قيام ألف عرض من جنس واحد وأكثر بمحل لا أثر لها إلا ما لواحد منها، وهذا كله محال. ومن دأب المعتزلة عدم المبالاة بالانسلاخ عن الدين، وارتكاب ما يأباه العقل وينادي بإحالته ويحكم على قائله بالتجاهل والخروج عن المعارف عند رجائهم نصرة ما هم عليه من الباطل، ودفع ما يتوجه عليهم من الحجج الهادفة لقواعد ما هم عليه من الضلال الآتية على ما يتمسكون به من البدع بالإبطال والاستئصال والرجوع إلى الطريق المستبين والصراط المستقيم خير من الإمعان في المهاوي والمهالك والإيجاف في البوادي والمجاهل، ونعوذ بالله تعالى من اتباع الوساوس والوقوع في ترهات البسابس
The effect would be non-existent if He were to be non-existent; He would not be a cause by affirmation. And if He were, it would not be through it, so one of them would not be necessitating emergence, so it would not be motion.
He verifies this: that this part did not traverse except one place, and if it were permissible for you to say: 'Two motions came to exist due to the existence of two movers,' it would be permissible for another to say: 'No, rather one motion came to exist due to the unity of the place that it traversed.' And it is said to him: If the existence of two motions that do not traverse except one place were permissible, then the existence of a thousand motions that do not traverse except one place would be permissible, and he is committed here.
And he says: If a thousand or more persons moved a body, there would occur in each of its parts motions equal to the number of agents. The statement that a thousand motions exist that do not traverse except one place is ignorance. And if it were permissible for anyone to imagine this, it would be permissible for another to say the opposite, and that a thousand places could be traversed by one motion. All of this is nonsense. So their corrupt principles led them to sometimes allow the subsistence of one accident in a thousand or more substrates, and sometimes allow the subsistence of a thousand accidents of one kind or more in a substrate with no effect except that of one of them. All of this is impossible. It is the habit of the Mu'tazila to be unconcerned with stripping off religion, and to commit what the intellect rejects and declares impossible and deems its speaker ignorant and exiting knowledge, when they hope to support the falsehood they are upon and to repel the proofs directed against them that smash the foundations of the misguidance they are upon, which obliterate and uproot the innovations they cling to. Returning to the clear path and the straight path is better than persisting in precipices and destructions and agitation in deserts and wastes. And we seek refuge with Allah Almighty from following whisperings and falling into the nonsense of the basabis.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position that multiple motions can occur in the same place. Nasafi begins with a conditional statement about causality: an effect would not exist if the cause were non-existent, but if the cause were not a cause by affirmation, then the effect would not come about. He then verifies this through an example: a moving part traverses only one place. If one claims two motions exist due to two movers, another could equally claim one motion due to the unity of the place. The argument is extended: allowing two motions in one place would permit a thousand motions in one place, which is absurd. Nasafi then presents the Mu'tazila response that a thousand agents moving a body would produce a thousand motions in each part, but he rejects this as ignorance, arguing that if one motion can traverse a thousand places, the opposite is equally absurd. He concludes that their corrupt principles lead to contradictory allowances: sometimes a single accident can subsist in multiple substrates, sometimes multiple accidents of the same kind subsist in one substrate with no cumulative effect. This is impossible. He criticizes the Mu'tazila for abandoning religion and committing what reason deems impossible, driven by the desire to defend falsehood. He ends with a prayer for guidance and protection from such deviations.

Key Terms
حركة
علة
محل
عرض
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple agents can produce multiple independent motions in the same object at the same time.
Correction: Nasafi argues that allowing two motions in one place leads to allowing a thousand motions, which is absurd. Therefore, multiple agents cannot produce multiple motions in the same place; this contradicts reason and causality.

Checkpoint
What does Nasafi argue would be the consequence of allowing two motions to exist in the same place?
A) It would prove the existence of a single creator.
B) It would allow a thousand motions in the same place, which is absurd.
C) It would confirm the Mu'tazila view of motion.
D) It would show that motion is a substance.

Key Takeaway
Nasafi refutes multiple motions in one place by showing that it leads to absurd consequences that contradict reason and Mu'tazila's own principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Nasafi warns against clinging to falsehood for the sake of supporting one's group. Have you ever stubbornly held a belief because admitting error would cost you respect among peers? Are you willing to examine your most cherished positions with the same rigor, even if it means returning to the clear path?
?
The Mu'tazila are described as unconcerned with stripping off religion to defend their principles. What conviction do you hold that, if proven wrong, would shake your identity? Are you prepared to accept truth even if it requires abandoning familiar ideas?
?
Nasafi seeks refuge from 'whispers' that lead to deviation. What internal whisper prevents you from submitting completely to what you know is true? Will you today consciously choose repentance over rationalization?

The Maturidi Distinction: One Act, Two Agents in Different Senses
Nasafi rebuts the Mu'tazila objection with a distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Nasafi refutes the Mu'tazila objection by distinguishing between God's act as an eternal attribute and the servant's act as God's effect, and by using generic and specific names.

Core Idea
One act can be attributed to both God and the servant, but in different senses.

In this lesson, Nasafi directly addresses the Mu'tazila argument that if one act could have two agents, then one statement could have two speakers. He clarifies that the Maturidi position never claims that one act is the act of two agents in the same sense. Instead, they distinguish between the ontological status of the act as God's creation and its attribution to the servant by generic and specific names.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial rebuttal
Read the Mu'tazila objection and note how Nasafi says it does not apply to the Maturidis because they never claim one act is the act of two agents absolutely.
The objection is based on a logical consistency argument.
2
Identify the distinction
Find where Nasafi distinguishes between what is God's act (eternal attribute) and what is the servant's act (maf'ul, effect). Understand that they are different in essence.
The servant's act is not God's act in the same sense.
3
Understand generic vs specific names
Notice how the term 'act' is a general name with specific types. God creates the type, but it is called the servant's act by generic name.
Example: If God creates the servant's speech, it is God's maf'ul and the servant's statement by specific name.

ومما يصولون على خصومهم بقولهم : لوجاز أن يكون فعل واحد فعلاً لفاعلين جاز أن يكون قول واحد قولاً لقائلين . يبطل بهذا أيضًا على ما قررنا أن الحركة الواحدة عندهم فعل لفاعلين ولم ينفعهم التجاهل ثم نقول : إن هذا الكلام لا يتوجه علينا فإنا لا نقول إن فعلاً واحدًا يكون فعلاً لفاعلين البتة، فإن ما هو فعل الله تعالى هو صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته وليس بفعل للعبد وما هو فعل للعبد ليس بفعل الله تعالى بل هو مفعوله، ثم الفعل اسم عام وله أسماء وأنواع ، فإن كان ما خلقه الله تعالى من فعل العبد ضربًا فهو معقول الله تعالى وفعل العبد باعتبار اسم جنسه و ضربه باعتبار اسم نوعه، وكذا لو خلق قول العبد فهو مفعول الله تعالى وهو فعل العبد باعتبار اسم جنسه وقوله باعتبار اسم نوعه، وكذا على قول أبي عيسى البرغوث وأبي حسن الأشعري لا يتوجه الإلزام فإنهما لا يجعلان فعلاً واحد لفاعلين بل لا فعل إلا لله تعالى، فأما العبد فله الكسب وقول العبد اسم لكسبه فيكون قوله فعلاً الله تعالى كسبًا للعبد، ثم ما هو من الأسماء للكسب يكون راجعا إلى من قام به الكسب فإن كان ذلك ضربًا فالضارب من قام به لا من خلقه والمتحرك من قامت به الحركة لا من خلقها، و القائل من قام به القول لا من خلقه، ولا يتصور قيام شيء من هذه المعاني إلا بمحل واحد فلا يوصف به اثنان، فأما الاتصاف بكونه فاعلاً ليس من شرطه قيام الفعل بالفاعل عندهم، ويحتجون عليكم في ذلك بالحركة والصفات الضرورية، فإن الفاعل للحركة الضرورية خالقها والمتحرك بها محلها، ولا يتصور اتصاف ذاتين بأنهما متحركان بحركة واحدة لاستحالة قياسها بذاتين، وكذا هذا في السواد فإن
And among what they assault their opponents with is their saying: 'If it were permissible for one act to be an act of two agents, it would be permissible for one statement to be a statement of two speakers.' He invalidates this also, based on what we have established, that the single motion according to them is an act of two agents, and ignorance did not benefit them. Then we say: This speech does not apply to us, for we do not say at all that one act is an act of two agents. Indeed, what is the act of Allah Almighty is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence and is not an act of the servant, and what is an act of the servant is not an act of Allah Almighty but rather is His effect. Then 'act' is a general name and it has names and types. If what Allah Almighty created of the servant's act is a type, then it is an intelligible of Allah Almighty and an act of the servant by virtue of its generic name and its type by virtue of its specific name. Likewise, if He created the servant's statement, it is an effect of Allah Almighty and it is an act of the servant by virtue of its generic name and his statement by virtue of its specific name. Likewise, according to the saying of Abu Isa al-Barghuth and Abu Hasan al-Ash'ari, the necessary consequence does not apply, for they do not make one act for two agents; rather, there is no act except for Allah Almighty. As for the servant, he has acquisition (al-kasb), and the servant's statement is a name for his acquisition. So his statement is an act of Allah Almighty and an acquisition of the servant. Then what gives names for acquisition refers to the one in whom the acquisition subsists. If that is beating, the beater is the one in whom it subsists, not the one who created it. The mover is the one in whom motion subsists, not the one who created it. The speaker is the one in whom speech subsists, not the one who created it. And it is not conceivable for any of these meanings to subsist except in a single substrate, so two cannot be described by it. As for being described as an agent, the subsistence of the act in the agent is not a condition according to them. And they argue against you in that by motion and necessary attributes: the agent of necessary motion is its creator, and the one moved by it is its locus. And it is not conceivable for two essences to be described as moving with one motion due to the impossibility of its subsistence in two essences, and likewise in the case of blackness, for...
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit where Nasafi responds to a Mu'tazila argument. The Mu'tazila argue: if one act can be the act of two agents, then one statement could be the statement of two speakers. They use this to challenge the Maturidi view that one motion can be the act of both God and the servant. Nasafi notes that this argument is posed against opponents, and he has already refuted the claim that one motion is an act of two agents (referring to the previous lesson). He then gives the Maturidi response: this argument does not apply to them because they never claim that one act is the act of two agents absolutely. What is God's act is an eternal attribute in His essence, not an act of the servant. What is the servant's act is not an act of God but rather His effect (maf'ul). The term 'act' (fi'l) is a general name with different specific names and types. God creates the servant's act as a type, but it is attributed to the servant by its generic name. For example, if God creates the servant's speech, that speech is God's creation (maf'ul Allah) and is also called the servant's act by generic name, and the servant's statement by specific name. Nasafi then mentions that according to Abu Isa al-Barghuth and Abu Hasan al-Ash'ari, the objection also fails. They hold that there is no act except from God; the servant has acquisition (kasb). The servant's statement is a name for his acquisition, so it is God's act (in creation) and the servant's acquisition (in acquisition). The names for acquisition refer to the one in whom the acquisition subsists—the striker is the one in whom the striking subsists, not the one who created it, etc. These meanings can only subsist in one substrate, so two cannot be described by them. Finally, he addresses the condition of being an agent: according to the Ash'arites, the act does not have to subsist in the agent. They use the example of necessary motion: the agent of necessary motion is its creator, and the one moved is its locus. Two essences cannot share one motion because it cannot subsist in two, and the same holds for blackness. The passage is cut off here.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God creates an action, the servant cannot be its agent at all.
Correction: Nasafi clarifies that God's act is an eternal attribute, while the servant's act is God's effect. The same entity can be called an act of both by different names: generic vs specific.

Checkpoint
According to Nasafi, why does the Mu'tazila objection about one act for two agents not apply to the Maturidi position?
A) Because Maturidis deny that any act can be attributed to the servant.
B) Because they distinguish between God's creating act and the servant's act by generic and specific names.
C) Because they accept that one act can be from two agents without distinction.
D) Because they agree with the Mu'tazila on the matter.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila objection fails because the Maturidis attribute one act to God and servant in different senses: God as creator, servant as locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, do you see it as solely your achievement or as an effect created by Allah? How does Nasafi's distinction between generic and specific act change the way you view your own role in your actions? Take a moment to sincerely thank Allah for enabling that deed.
?
The Mu'tazila's objection stemmed from a desire to preserve human justice. Have you ever felt a tension between your own responsibility and your belief that Allah controls everything? Let Nasafi's clarification that one act can be attributed to two agents in different senses settle that conflict in your heart, and commit to relying on Allah while still striving.
?
Think of a time you blamed yourself harshly for a sin. Does recognizing that the sin is also an effect of Allah's creation make you more humble and hopeful in seeking forgiveness? Or does it tempt you to shirk responsibility? Reflect on how you can hold yourself accountable while trusting in Allah's mercy.
